Actually vectors C and D are points on line MN, which is a line not through origin. So they are not multiples of each other (they are linearly independent). So we can solve for P.

Thus we have:

P = (some vector) satisfying P·C = |C|^2/2, P·D = |D|^2/2.

We can write P in terms of basis {C, D}. Since C and D are linearly independent, we can set P = α C + β D? Actually we need to solve linear system. Alternatively, we can find P as the point such that P is the circumcenter. There is a known formula: For triangle with vertices at vectors 0, C, D, the circumcenter is given by (|C|^2 D - |D|^2 C) × (C × D) / (2|C×D|^2) in 3D? Actually in 2D, we can use the fact that the circumcenter is the solution to the linear system:

P·C = |C|^2/2,
P·D = |D|^2/2.

We can solve for P by writing P = u C + v D? But that's not straightforward because we need dot products. So we can compute P explicitly in terms of C and D. Since C and D are known in terms of M,N,r,R, we can compute P.

Then H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Points: P, M, N. Since M and N are on line MN, we have triangle with base MN. The orthocenter H can be computed as the intersection of altitude from P to MN (which is line through P perpendicular to MN) and altitude from M to PN, etc. Since we have coordinates, we can compute H.

We can compute the circumcircle of BEF, and then show that the distance from its center to the line y=y_H equals its radius. Alternatively, we can show that the line y=y_H is the polar of something relative to that circle, or that H lies on the Simson line of some point.

Equivalently, y_O^2 - 2y_O y_H + y_H^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 + y_O^2 => -2y_O y_H + y_H^2 = (n_x - m_x)^2 => 2y_O y_H = y_H^2 - (n_x - m_x)^2. So y_O = (y_H^2 - (n_x - m_x)^2) / (2y_H), provided y_H ≠ 0. Now we need to compute P from linear system:

P·C = |C|^2/2,
P·D = |D|^2/2.

We can treat P as unknown vector. Since C and D are not parallel (line MN not through origin), we can solve.

Let’s compute C and D explicitly.

Now, |C|^2 = C·C. Since C is on Ω, we know that distance from M to C is r, but we have M = (m_x,m_y). So |C-M| = r. That gives |C|^2 - 2M·C + |M|^2 = r^2. But |M|^2 = r^2. So |C|^2 - 2M·C + r^2 = r^2 => |C|^2 = 2M·C. So indeed, |C|^2 = 2M·C. Similarly, for D: |D|^2 = 2N·D? Check: D is on Γ, so |D-N| = R. Then |D|^2 - 2N·D + |N|^2 = R^2. Since |N|^2=R^2, we get |D|^2 = 2N·D. So indeed, |C|^2 = 2M·C, |D|^2 = 2N·D. That is nice.

Thus the equations for P become:

P·C = M·C, (since |C|^2/2 = M·C)
P·D = N·D.

So P satisfies:

P·C = M·C,
P·D = N·D.

Let’s do it systematically with complex numbers: Let’s denote P, C, D as complex numbers. Thus we have two linear equations in P (real and imaginary parts). Solve.

We can treat P as unknown complex, and write the two equations as:

P conj(C) + C conj(P) = |C|^2,
P conj(D) + D conj(P) = |D|^2.

These are linear in P and conj(P). We can solve for P by considering them as equations in the real and imaginary parts. But we can also find P by using the fact that the circumcenter is given by:

But then Re( X conj(C) ) = α |C|^2 + β Re( D conj(C) ) = |C|^2/2. Similarly, Re( X conj(D) ) = α Re( C conj(D) ) + β |D|^2 = |D|^2/2. So we have:

α |C|^2 + β (Re( D conj(C) )) = |C|^2/2,
α (Re( C conj(D) )) + β |D|^2 = |D|^2/2.

Note that Re( D conj(C) ) = Re( C conj(D) ) = C·D (dot product). So let’s denote:

S = |C|^2, T = |D|^2, U = C·D (real). Then we have:

α S + β U = S/2,
α U + β T = T/2.

Solve for α, β. Then P = α C + β D.

Thus α and β satisfy:

α S + β U = S/2 => α = (S/2 - β U)/S = 1/2 - (β U)/S.
Plug into second: (1/2 - (β U)/S) U + β T = T/2 => U/2 - (β U^2)/S + β T = T/2 => β (T - U^2/S) = (T - U)/2 => β = (T - U)/2 / (T - U^2/S) = (T - U)/2 * S/(S T - U^2) = S(T - U)/(2(ST - U^2)).

Similarly, α = 1/2 - (U/S) β = 1/2 - (U/S) * S(T - U)/(2(ST - U^2)) = 1/2 - U(T - U)/(2(ST - U^2)) = ( (ST - U^2) - U(T - U) ) / (2(ST - U^2)) = (ST - U^2 - U T + U^2) / (2(ST - U^2)) = (ST - U T) / (2(ST - U^2)) = T(S - U) / (2(ST - U^2)).

Thus:

α = T(S - U) / (2(ST - U^2)),
β = S(T - U) / (2(ST - U^2)).

Then P = α C + β D.

Now, note that ST - U^2 = |C|^2|D|^2 - (C·D)^2 = |C × D|^2 (the squared area). So that is positive.

Given the algebraic nature, we can compute the equation of circle through B, E, F. Since E and F are on line AP, we might find the circle's equation in terms of λ. There is a known formula: The circle through B and two points on a line L (AP) is such that the power of A with respect to that circle is something. But we can compute directly.

Let’s denote the coordinates of points on AP as (λ X, 1 + λ (Y-1)). Let’s denote u = X, w = Y-1 (so direction v = (u, w)). Then param: (λ u, 1+λ w). Then A is λ=0, P is λ=1.

Now, we have E = λ_E = s_E, F = λ_F = s_F.

We also have B = (0,-1).

We need to find the circumcircle of triangle with vertices B, (λ_E u, 1+λ_E w), (λ_F u, 1+λ_F w). This is a circle that can be described by an equation of the form: x^2+y^2 + Ax + By + C = 0.

Since B is (0,-1), plugging gives: 0+1 + 0 + B*(-1) + C = 0 => 1 - B + C = 0 => C = B - 1.

Now, the other two points satisfy:

(λ_E u)^2 + (1+λ_E w)^2 + A (λ_E u) + B (1+λ_E w) + C = 0,
(λ_F u)^2 + (1+λ_F w)^2 + A (λ_F u) + B (1+λ_F w) + C = 0.

Subtract the two equations to eliminate C? Actually we can solve for A and B.

Let’s denote for a given λ: F(λ) = λ^2 u^2 + (1+λ w)^2 + A λ u + B (1+λ w) + C = 0.

Simplify: λ^2(u^2+w^2) + 2λ w + 1 + A λ u + B + B λ w + C = 0 => λ^2 (u^2+w^2) + λ(2w + A u + B w) + (1 + B + C) = 0.

So for λ = λ_E and λ_F, we have:

λ_E^2 K + λ_E L + M = 0,
λ_F^2 K + λ_F L + M = 0,

where K = u^2+w^2 = X^2+(Y-1)^2, L = 2w + A u + B w, M = 1 + B + C.

Note that C = B - 1, so M = 1 + B + (B - 1) = 2B. So M = 2B.

Thus we have:

λ_E^2 K + λ_E L + 2B = 0,
λ_F^2 K + λ_F L + 2B = 0.

Subtract: K(λ_E^2 - λ_F^2) + L(λ_E - λ_F) = 0 => (λ_E - λ_F)[K(λ_E+λ_F) + L] = 0. Since λ_E ≠ λ_F (unless s_E=s_F which would imply something degenerate), we have:

K(λ_E+λ_F) + L = 0 => L = -K(λ_E+λ_F).

Now, plug back into one equation: λ_E^2 K + λ_E (-K(λ_E+λ_F)) + 2B = 0 => K(λ_E^2 - λ_E^2 - λ_E λ_F) + 2B = 0 => -K λ_E λ_F + 2B = 0 => 2B = K λ_E λ_F => B = (K λ_E λ_F)/2.

Thus we have determined A and B in terms of K and λ_E, λ_F. Actually we only got B. We also have L = -K(λ_E+λ_F) = 2w + A u + B w. So we can solve for A:

2w + A u + B w = -K(λ_E+λ_F) => A u = -K(λ_E+λ_F) - 2w - B w => A = [ -K(λ_E+λ_F) - w(2+B) ] / u.

Given two intersecting circles, the line joining their centers is the perpendicular bisector of the common chord. The circumcenter of triangle formed by one intersection point and the two points where the line of centers meets the circles is something like the midpoint of the arc? There is a known fact: For two intersecting circles, the radical axis is AB. The points C and D are the second intersections of the line of centers with the circles. Then the circumcenter of triangle ACD is the midpoint of the arc? Let's examine.

Given the algebraic simplifications, perhaps we can compute the circle (BEF) and then compute the distance from O to line L. Since we have a=1, we can express everything in terms of m,n. Let's try to compute λ_E and λ_F.

X = (c+d)/2, so X^2 = (c+d)^2/4.
Y-1 = (cd-1)/2, so (Y-1)^2 = (cd-1)^2/4.

s_F = (n(c+d) - cd +1) / K.

Now, we have K = (c^2+d^2 + c^2 d^2 +1)/4.

So s_E = 4(m(c+d) - cd +1) / (c^2+d^2 + c^2 d^2 +1).
s_F = 4(n(c+d) - cd +1) / (c^2+d^2 + c^2 d^2 +1).

Let's compute c and d explicitly using hyperbolic functions? Since we have m and n real, we can set m = sinh u? Actually sqrt(m^2+1) = cosh u if m = sinh u. Write D = 1 + e^{-2u} + e^{2v} + e^{2v-2u} = (1 + e^{-2u}) + e^{2v}(1 + e^{-2u}) = (1+e^{-2u})(1+e^{2v})? Thus s_E = 2(1+e^{v+u})/(1+e^{2v}).

From earlier, s_F = (n(c+d) - cd +1)/K. With n = sinh v, c = -e^{-u}, d = e^{v}. So we need to compute n(c+d) - cd +1.

We have n = (e^v - e^{-v})/2.
c+d = -e^{-u} + e^{v}.
So n(c+d) = (e^v - e^{-v})/2 * ( -e^{-u}+e^{v} ) = (1/2)( (e^v - e^{-v})(-e^{-u}+e^{v}) ). Expand: (e^v)(-e^{-u}) = -e^{v-u}, (e^v)(e^{v}) = e^{2v}, (-e^{-v})(-e^{-u}) = e^{-u-v}, (-e^{-v})(e^{v}) = -1. Sum: -e^{v-u} + e^{2v} + e^{-u-v} -1. So numerator = (1/2)( 1 + e^{v-u} + e^{2v} + e^{-u-v} ). So s_F = ( numerator ) / K = (1/2)(1+e^{v-u}+e^{2v}+e^{-u-v}) / K.

So K = D/4.

Thus s_F = (1/2)(1+e^{v-u}+e^{2v}+e^{-u-v}) / (D/4) = 2 (1+e^{v-u}+e^{2v}+e^{-u-v}) / D.

Given the complexity, maybe we can use coordinates in the hyperbolic parametrization we found: u and v. That gave simple expressions for C and D: C = (-e^{-u},0), D = (e^{v},0). We have:

c = -e^{-u}
d = e^{v}
X = (c+d)/2 = (e^{v} - e^{-u})/2
Y = (cd+1)/2 = (-e^{v-u} + 1)/2 = (1 - e^{v-u})/2

Let’s denote:

A = (0,1)
P = (X, Y)

Now, K = X^2 + (1+e^{v-u})^2/4 = (e^{v} - e^{-u})^2/4 + (1+e^{v-u})^2/4 = (1/4)[ e^{2v} - 2e^{v-u} + e^{-2u} + 1 + 2e^{v-u} + e^{2(v-u)} ] = (1/4)[ e^{2v} + e^{-2u} + 1 + e^{2(v-u)} ] = (1/4)(1+e^{-2u}+e^{2v}+e^{2(v-u)}). So K = D/4, as before.

Now, s_E = 2(mX - (Y-1))/K. But m = sinh u = (e^u - e^{-u})/2. So mX = (e^u - e^{-u})/2 * (e^v - e^{-u})/2 = (1/4)(e^u - e^{-u})(e^v - e^{-u}). Expand: (e^u)(e^v) = e^{u+v}, (e^u)(-e^{-u}) = -1, (-e^{-u})(e^v) = -e^{v-u}, (-e^{-u})(-e^{-u}) = e^{-2u}. Sum: e^{u+v} -1 - e^{v-u} + e^{-2u}. So mX = (1/4)( e^{u+v} -1 - e^{v-u} + e^{-2u} ). Then mX - (Y-1) = mX - ( -(1+e^{v-u})/2 )? Actually Y-1 = (1 - e^{v-u})/2 -1 = (1 - e^{v-u} -2)/2 = -(1+e^{v-u})/2. So mX - (Y-1) = mX + (1+e^{v-u})/2 = (1/4)( e^{u+v} -1 - e^{v-u} + e^{-2u} ) + (1/2)(1+e^{v-u}) = combine: (1/4)( e^{u+v} -1 - e^{v-u} + e^{-2u} ) + (2/4)(1+e^{v-u}) = (1/4)( e^{u+v} -1 - e^{v-u} + e^{-2u} + 2 + 2e^{v-u} ) = (1/4)( e^{u+v} + 1 + e^{v-u} + e^{-2u} ). So mX - (Y-1) = (1/4)( e^{u+v} + 1 + e^{v-u} + e^{-2u} ). Then s_E = 2 * (1/4)( e^{u+v} + 1 + e^{v-u} + e^{-2u} ) / (D/4) = 2 * (1/4)( ... ) * (4/D) = 2 ( e^{u+v} + 1 + e^{v-u} + e^{-2u} ) / D. But earlier we derived s_E = 2(1+e^{u+v})/(1+e^{2v})? That's different. Let's compute D = (1+e^{-2u})(1+e^{2v}) = 1+e^{-2u}+e^{2v}+e^{2v-2u}. So s_E = 2( e^{u+v} + 1 + e^{v-u} + e^{-2u} ) / (1+e^{-2u}+e^{2v}+e^{2v-2u}). This is messy.

Maybe we can find a simpler expression by using the fact that s_E = 2 (1+e^{u+v})/(1+e^{2v})? For u=0,v=1, that gave 2*(1+2.718)/(1+7.389)=2*3.718/8.389=0.886, but our computed s_E from earlier was 0.887, so maybe that's actually the same? Let's compute s_E from our new expression with u=0,v=1: D = (1+1)(1+e^2)=2*8.389=16.778, numerator: e^{0+1}+1+e^{1-0}+e^{0}= e^1+1+e^1+1=2e+2=2*2.718+2=5.436+2=7.436, so s_E = 2*7.436/16.778=14.872/16.778=0.886, matches. So s_E = 2( e^{u+v}+1+e^{v-u}+e^{-2u})/D simplifies to 2(1+e^{u+v})/(1+e^{2v})? Let's test: For u=0, e^{u+v}=e^v, 1+e^{u+v}=1+e^v. And 2(1+e^v)/(1+e^{2v}) = 2(1+2.718)/(1+7.389)=2*3.718/8.389=7.436/8.389=0.886. So indeed s_E = 2(1+e^{u+v})/(1+e^{2v}). So our expression simplifies to that. But we need to verify algebraically that:

2(e^{u+v}+1+e^{v-u}+e^{-2u}) / ( (1+e^{-2u})(1+e^{2v}) ) = 2(1+e^{u+v})/(1+e^{2v}). This would imply:

(e^{u+v}+1+e^{v-u}+e^{-2u}) / (1+e^{-2u}) = 1+e^{u+v}. Multiply both sides: e^{u+v}+1+e^{v-u}+e^{-2u} = (1+e^{u+v})(1+e^{-2u}) = 1+e^{-2u}+e^{u+v}+e^{u+v-2u} = 1+e^{-2u}+e^{u+v}+e^{v-u}. So indeed, left side = right side. So s_E = 2(1+e^{u+v})/(1+e^{2v}). Good.

Similarly, for s_F: s_F = 2(1+e^{v-u}+e^{2v}+e^{-u-v})/D. We want to see if this simplifies to something like 2(1+e^{u+v})/(1+e^{2v})? That would give s_F = s_E, but we know s_F is different. Let's try to simplify s_F expression using similar factorization: s_F = 2(1+e^{v-u}+e^{2v}+e^{-u-v})/( (1+e^{-2u})(1+e^{2v}) ). Write numerator = (1+e^{2v}) + e^{v-u}+e^{-u-v} = (1+e^{2v}) + e^{-u}(e^{v}+e^{-v}). Then s_F = 2[ (1+e^{2v}) + e^{-u}(e^{v}+e^{-v}) ] / [ (1+e^{-2u})(1+e^{2v}) ] = 2/(1+e^{-2u}) + 2e^{-u}(e^{v}+e^{-v})/( (1+e^{-2u})(1+e^{2v}) ). Now, 2/(1+e^{-2u}) = 2e^{2u}/(1+e^{2u}). And the second term: 2e^{-u}(e^{v}+e^{-v})/( (1+e^{-2u})(1+e^{2v}) ) = 2e^{-u}(e^{v}+e^{-v})/( (1+e^{-2u})(1+e^{2v}) ). Write 1+e^{-2u} = e^{-2u}(1+e^{2u}), so denominator = e^{-2u}(1+e^{2u})(1+e^{2v}). Then term becomes 2e^{-u}(e^{v}+e^{-v}) * e^{2u} / ((1+e^{2u})(1+e^{2v})) = 2e^{u}(e^{v}+e^{-v})/((1+e^{2u})(1+e^{2v})). So s_F = 2e^{2u}/(1+e^{2u}) + 2e^{u}(e^{v}+e^{-v})/((1+e^{2u})(1+e^{2v})). That doesn't look like a simple exponential.

But maybe we can express s_F in terms of hyperbolic functions: s_F = 2 cosh(v-u) / (1+e^{2v})? Let's test with u=0,v=1: 2 cosh(1) = 2*1.543 = 3.086, divided by (1+e^2)=8.389 gives 0.367, not 1.369. So no.

Alternatively, s_F = 2e^{u+v}/(1+e^{2v})? For u=0,v=1: 2e^1/8.389=5.436/8.389=0.647, no.

Given the numeric, s_F ≈ 1.369. Perhaps s_F = 2(1+e^{v+u})/(1+e^{2v}) + something? Actually note that s_E = 2(1+e^{u+v})/(1+e^{2v}) and s_F = 2(1+e^{v-u}+e^{2v}+e^{-u-v})/D. Since D = (1+e^{-2u})(1+e^{2v}) = e^{-2u}(1+e^{2u})(1+e^{2v}), we can write s_F = 2(1+e^{v-u}+e^{2v}+e^{-u-v}) * e^{2u} / ((1+e^{2u})(1+e^{2v})) = 2( e^{2u} + e^{u+v} + e^{2v+2u} + e^{u-v} ) / ((1+e^{2u})(1+e^{2v})). That is symmetric in u and v? Not exactly.

Maybe we can find that s_F = 2(1+e^{v+u})/(1+e^{2v}) + 2e^{u+v}/(1+e^{2v})? That would be 2(1+2e^{u+v})/(1+e^{2v}) which for u=0,v=1 gives 2(1+2*2.718)/8.389=2(1+5.436)/8.389=2*6.436/8.389=12.872/8.389=1.536, close to 1.369 but not exactly.

Let's compute s_F exactly using our derived expression: s_F = 2(1+e^{v-u}+e^{2v}+e^{-u-v}) / D. Since D = (1+e^{-2u})(1+e^{2v}) = 1+e^{-2u}+e^{2v}+e^{2v-2u}. We can also write s_F = 2(1+e^{2v}+e^{v-u}+e^{-u-v}) / D. Now, note that (1+e^{2v}) = e^{2v}(1+e^{-2v}) = e^{2v}(1+e^{-2v}). So s_F = 2e^{2v}(1+e^{-2v}+e^{-u-v}+e^{-u-2v}) / D? Not.

Given the algebraic mess, maybe we can find a more clever parametrization. Since we have u and v, we can also set t = e^{u} and s = e^{v}. Then u = ln t, v = ln s, with t>0, s>0. Then:

c = -1/t, d = s.
X = (s - 1/t)/2.
Y = (1 - s/t)/2? Because e^{v-u} = s/t. So Y = (1 - s/t)/2.
Then direction v = (X, Y-1) = ( (s - 1/t)/2, (1 - s/t)/2 - 1 ) = ( (s - 1/t)/2, (1 - s/t - 2)/2 ) = ( (s - 1/t)/2, (-1 - s/t)/2 ) = ( (s - 1/t)/2, -(1 + s/t)/2 ).

Now, m = sinh u = (t - 1/t)/2. n = sinh v = (s - 1/s)/2.

Now, compute s_E = 2(mX - (Y-1))/K. But we already have a nice expression: s_E = 2(1+e^{u+v})/(1+e^{2v}) = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2). Since e^{u+v}=st, e^{2v}=s^2. So s_E = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2). Similarly, we can compute s_F using the expression we derived: s_F = 2(1+e^{v-u}+e^{2v}+e^{-u-v})/D. Let's write in terms of s,t: e^{v-u}=s/t, e^{2v}=s^2, e^{-u-v}=1/(st). So numerator = 1 + s/t + s^2 + 1/(st). Denominator D = (1+e^{-2u})(1+e^{2v}) = (1+1/t^2)(1+s^2) = ((t^2+1)/t^2)(1+s^2) = (t^2+1)(1+s^2)/t^2. So s_F = 2(1 + s/t + s^2 + 1/(st)) / ((t^2+1)(1+s^2)/t^2) = 2t^2(1 + s/t + s^2 + 1/(st)) / ((t^2+1)(1+s^2)) = 2t^2(1 + s^2) + 2t^2(s/t + 1/(st))? Actually compute: t^2(1) = t^2, t^2(s/t)=st, t^2(s^2)=s^2 t^2, t^2(1/(st))=t/s. So numerator becomes 2( t^2 + st + s^2 t^2 + t/s ). So s_F = 2( t^2 + st + s^2 t^2 + t/s ) / ((t^2+1)(1+s^2)). That is s_F = 2( t^2 + st + s^2 t^2 + t/s ) / ((1+t^2)(1+s^2)). This seems symmetric if we swap t and s? Not exactly because t/s appears.

We can also write s_F = 2( (t^2 + s^2 t^2) + (st + t/s) ) / ((1+t^2)(1+s^2)) = 2( t^2(1+s^2) + t(s + 1/s) ) / ((1+t^2)(1+s^2)) = 2t^2/(1+t^2) + 2t(s+1/s)/((1+t^2)(1+s^2)). That is similar to earlier expression.

Now, note that s_E = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2). So s_E and s_F are different.

Now, we also have B = (0,-1). So triangle BEF: B=(0,-1), E=(s_E X, 1+s_E (Y-1)), F=(s_F X, 1+s_F (Y-1)). Since we have parametric forms, we can compute the circle (BEF). Perhaps we can find that the circle (BEF) is orthogonal to something, and then the line through H parallel to AP is the radical axis of two circles, and then tangency follows.

Given the algebraic simplification, maybe we can compute the equation of circle (BEF) and then compute its center O and radius, and then compute the distance from O to line L (through H parallel to AP). Then verify that the distance equals radius. This is doable with computer algebra, but we need to do it manually. However, we can attempt to simplify using the fact that we have only two parameters t and s (with t = e^u, s = e^v). And we have derived expressions for X, Y, s_E, s_F, etc. Perhaps we can find that the circle (BEF) is actually the circle with diameter something like B and the point where AP meets something? Or maybe its center is at (something, 0) and radius is something like (t^2+1)/2? Let's compute numeric: u=0,t=1,s=2.718? Actually s = e^1 ≈2.718, t=1. Then compute:

X = (s - 1/t)/2 = (2.718 - 1)/2 = 0.859
Y = (1 - s/t)/2 = (1 - 2.718)/2 = -0.859
s_E = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2) = 2(1+2.718)/ (1+7.389)=2*3.718/8.389=7.436/8.389=0.886
s_F = 2( t^2 + st + s^2 t^2 + t/s ) / ((1+t^2)(1+s^2)) = 2(1 + 2.718 + 7.389 + 1/2.718) / ((2)(8.389)) = 2(1+2.718+7.389+0.3679) / (16.778) = 2(11.4749)/16.778=22.9498/16.778=1.369

Now, E = (s_E X, 1+s_E (Y-1)) = (0.886*0.859, 1+0.886*(-1.859)) = (0.761, 1-1.649) = (0.761, -0.649)
F = (1.369*0.859, 1+1.369*(-1.859)) = (1.174, 1-2.544) = (1.174, -1.544)
B = (0,-1)

Now, find circumcircle of BEF. Compute center O. Let's compute coordinates:

B(0,-1), E(0.761,-0.649), F(1.174,-1.544). We can find perpendicular bisector of BE and BF. Compute midpoints:

BE midpoint: ((0+0.761)/2, (-1-0.649)/2) = (0.3805, -0.8245). Slope BE: (-0.649+1)/(0.761-0)=0.351/0.761≈0.461, so perpendicular slope = -1/0.461≈-2.171. Equation: y+0.8245 = -2.171(x-0.3805). For BF: B(0,-1), F(1.174,-1.544). Midpoint: (0.587, -1.272). Slope BF: (-1.544+1)/(1.174-0)= -0.544/1.174≈-0.463, perpendicular slope = 1/0.463≈2.159. Equation: y+1.272 = 2.159(x-0.587). Solve intersection:

From first: y = -0.8245 -2.171(x-0.3805) = -0.8245 -2.171x +0.8245? Actually 2.171*0.3805≈0.8245, so y = -0.8245 -2.171x +0.8245 = -2.171x. So y = -2.171x.

From second: y = -1.272 + 2.159(x-0.587) = -1.272 + 2.159x -1.267 = 2.159x -2.539. Set equal: -2.171x = 2.159x -2.539 => -2.171x -2.159x = -2.539 => -4.33x = -2.539 => x = 0.586, y = -2.171*0.586 = -1.271. So O ≈ (0.586, -1.271). Radius = distance to B: sqrt((0.586)^2 + (-1.271+1)^2) = sqrt(0.343 + (-0.271)^2)= sqrt(0.343+0.0735)= sqrt(0.4165)=0.645. Note that the line through H parallel to AP: H = (X, h_y) = (0.859, h_y). h_y = (X-m)(n-X)/Y. m=0, n=sinh v = (s-1/s)/2 ≈ (2.718-0.3679)/2=2.3501/2=1.17505? Actually s=2.718, so 1/s=0.3679, n=(2.718-0.3679)/2=1.17505. Then n-X =1.17505-0.859=0.31605, X-m=0.859-0=0.859, Y=-0.859, so h_y = (0.859*0.31605)/(-0.859)=0.2715/(-0.859)= -0.3158. So H = (0.859, -0.3158). The direction v = (X, Y-1) = (0.859, -1.859). So line L through H with direction v: param (0.859+0.859t, -0.3158 -1.859t). We want to see if this line is tangent to circle (BEF). Compute distance from O to L. O = (0.586, -1.271). Direction d = (0.859, -1.859). Compute the distance from O to line through H with direction d. The distance = |(O-H) × d| / |d|. Compute O-H = (0.586-0.859, -1.271+0.3158) = (-0.273, -0.9552). Cross product with d: (-0.273)*(-1.859) - (-0.9552)*(0.859) = 0.507 + 0.820 = 1.327. |d| = sqrt(0.859^2+1.859^2)= sqrt(0.738+3.456)= sqrt(4.194)=2.048. So distance = 1.327/2.048 = 0.648, which matches radius 0.645 approximately. So tangency holds.

Thus we have strong numeric evidence. Now we need to prove it generally.

Given the algebraic complexity, perhaps we can find a more synthetic proof using properties of circles and orthocenters.

Let’s try to understand the geometry.

We have circles Ω and Γ intersecting at A and B. Let M and N be their centers. Let line MN intersect Ω at C (other than the point on the ray from M away from N) and Γ at D (other than the point on the ray from N away from M). So C and D are external to segment MN. So we have C-M-N-D collinear.

Let P be circumcenter of triangle ACD. So P is the center of the circle through A, C, D.

Line AP meets Ω again at E and Γ again at F.

We have H orthocenter of triangle PMN.

We need to prove: line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF).

Observations:

- Since A, C, D lie on circles Ω and Γ? Actually A and C on Ω, A and D on Γ. So triangle ACD has two sides that are chords of the circles. The circumcenter P of ACD is the point equidistant from A, C, D. There is a known relationship: P is the midpoint of the arc CD of the circle (ACD), but not helpful.

- Also, note that the points C and D are the intersections of the line MN with the circles. So the circle (ACD) is the circle with diameter? Possibly, the circle (ACD) is orthogonal to something? Let’s compute the power of M with respect to circle (ACD). Since M is center of Ω, and A and C are on Ω, we have MA=MC=r. Also, M lies on line MN, but not necessarily on circle (ACD). The power of M: Pow(M) = MA*? Actually if M is on the radical axis? Not.

- Consider the circumcircle of triangle PMN. Since H is orthocenter, we know that the reflection of H across MN lies on circumcircle of PMN. Also, the reflection of H across the midpoint of MN lies on the circumcircle? Not.

- The line through H parallel to AP: we want it to be tangent to circle (BEF). Since AP is a line through A, and H is related to P and M,N, maybe the line through H parallel to AP is the Simson line of point A with respect to triangle PMN? Let's test with numeric: For triangle PMN, we have points: P=(0.859,-0.859), M=(0,0), N=(1.175,0). The Simson line of A=(0,1) relative to triangle PMN: we need to compute feet of perpendiculars from A to the sides of triangle PMN. Compute side MN: line through M(0,0) and N(1.175,0) is horizontal y=0. Foot from A to MN is (0,0). Side PM: line through P and M. Compute PM vector: (0.859, -0.859). So line PM: through M(0,0) with direction (1,-1). Equation: y = -x. Foot from A(0,1) to line y=-x: line perpendicular has slope 1, through A: y-1 = 1(x-0) => y=x+1. Intersection with y=-x: -x = x+1 => -2x=1 => x=-0.5, y=0.5. So foot is (-0.5,0.5). Side PN: line through P and N. Compute PN vector: (1.175-0.859, 0 - (-0.859)) = (0.316, 0.859). So line PN: through N(1.175,0) with direction (0.316,0.859). Its equation: param (1.175+0.316t, 0+0.859t). We need foot from A(0,1). Compute using projection. The foot can be found by solving. Let’s compute: The vector from N to A: A-N = (-1.175,1). We want to find t such that (N + t d - A) dot d = 0, where d=(0.316,0.859). So (N - A + t d) dot d = 0 => ( (1.175-0, 0-1) + t d ) dot d = (1.175, -1) dot d + t |d|^2 = 0. Compute d dot (1.175,-1): 0.316*1.175 + 0.859*(-1)=0.371 -0.859 = -0.488. |d|^2 = 0.316^2+0.859^2=0.0998+0.738=0.8378. So t = 0.488/0.8378=0.582. Then foot = N + t d = (1.175+0.582*0.316, 0+0.582*0.859)= (1.175+0.184, 0.5)= (1.359,0.5). So feet: (0,0), (-0.5,0.5), (1.359,0.5). These three points are collinear? Check slope between first two: (0.5-0)/(-0.5-0)=0.5/(-0.5)=-1. Slope between first and third: (0.5-0)/(1.359-0)=0.5/1.359≈0.367, not equal. So they are not collinear. So A is not on the circumcircle of PMN? Actually for Simson line, the point must be on the circumcircle. So A is not on circumcircle of PMN. So that property doesn't hold.

But maybe there is another relation: The line through H parallel to AP might be the polar of A with respect to circle (BEF)? Or something like that.

Given the difficulty, I think a coordinate proof using the hyperbolic parametrization is viable, albeit messy. We can try to compute the circle (BEF) and the distance from its center to line L in terms of t and s, and simplify to show equality. Since we have explicit expressions for B, E, F in terms of t and s, we can compute the circumcenter O and radius, and then compute distance from O to line through H parallel to v. This will involve algebraic manipulation. But we can do it systematically.

Let’s set:

t = e^u > 0, s = e^v > 0. We have t < s? Since u < v, t < s? Actually e^u < e^v, yes.

Define:

X = (s - 1/t)/2
Y = (1 - s/t)/2 = (1 - (s/t))/2

Then v = (X, Y-1) = (X, - (1 + s/t)/2) because Y-1 = (1 - s/t)/2 - 1 = (1 - s/t - 2)/2 = -(1 + s/t)/2.

So direction d = (X, - (1 + s/t)/2).

Now, s_E = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2) (since e^{u+v}=st). And s_F = 2( t^2 + st + s^2 t^2 + t/s ) / ((1+t^2)(1+s^2)). But maybe we can find a simpler expression for s_F. Using the derived formula: s_F = 2(1 + s/t + s^2 + 1/(st)) / ( (1+1/t^2)(1+s^2) ) = 2(1 + s/t + s^2 + 1/(st)) * t^2 / ((t^2+1)(1+s^2)) = 2( t^2 + st + s^2 t^2 + t/s ) / ((t^2+1)(1+s^2)). That is fine.

Now, E = (s_E X, 1 + s_E (Y-1)) = (s_E X, 1 + s_E * ( - (1+s/t)/2 ) ) = (s_E X, 1 - s_E (1+s/t)/2).
Similarly, F = (s_F X, 1 - s_F (1+s/t)/2).

B = (0, -1).

Now, we need to find the circle through B, E, F. Let’s denote λ = s_E, μ = s_F for simplicity. Then:

E = (λ X, 1 - λ w), where w = (1+s/t)/2.
F = (μ X, 1 - μ w).
B = (0, -1).

Note that X and w are known in terms of s,t. Also, note that w = (1+s/t)/2, X = (s - 1/t)/2.

We can compute the circumcenter O. Since B is (0,-1), we might find O = (a, b). The circle equation: (x - a)^2 + (y - b)^2 = R^2.

We can compute using perpendicular bisectors. Let’s compute midpoints and slopes.

First, find the perpendicular bisector of BE. Coordinates:

B(0,-1), E(λX, 1 - λw). Let’s denote E = (E_x, E_y), F = (F_x, F_y). Then:

Midpoint BE: M_BE = ( (0+λX)/2, (-1+1-λw)/2 ) = ( λX/2, -λw/2 ). So M_BE = ( λX/2, -λw/2 ).

Slope of BE: (E_y + 1)/(E_x - 0) = (1 - λw + 1)/(λX) = (2 - λw)/(λX). So perpendicular slope = - (λX)/(2 - λw). So equation of perpendicular bisector of BE: (y - (-λw/2)) = - (λX)/(2 - λw) (x - λX/2). That is: y + λw/2 = - (λX)/(2 - λw) (x - λX/2).

Similarly, for BF: B(0,-1), F(μX, 1 - μw). Midpoint BF: ( μX/2, -μw/2 ). Slope BF: (1 - μw + 1)/(μX) = (2 - μw)/(μX). Perpendicular slope = - (μX)/(2 - μw). Equation: y + μw/2 = - (μX)/(2 - μw) (x - μX/2).

We need to find intersection O = (a,b) of these two lines. This looks messy but perhaps we can find a simpler relation.

Alternatively, we can find the circle using the fact that B, E, F are three points, and we can compute the circumcircle equation in terms of λ and μ. Since we have param, maybe we can find that the circle (BEF) is orthogonal to something like the circle with diameter something, and then the distance condition reduces to a simple identity.

Another approach: Use complex numbers and the concept of spiral similarity. The points B, E, F: B is the reflection of A across the line MN. The line AP is a line through A. There might be a homothety centered at A that sends circle (BEF) to some other circle. Or maybe the line through H parallel to AP is the radical axis of two circles.

Let's compute H coordinates in terms of s,t.

We have:

m = sinh u = (t - 1/t)/2.
n = sinh v = (s - 1/s)/2.

X = (s - 1/t)/2.
Y = (1 - s/t)/2.

We computed h_y = (X - m)(n - X)/Y.

Compute X - m = (s - 1/t)/2 - (t - 1/t)/2 = (s - 1/t - t + 1/t)/2 = (s - t)/2.
n - X = (s - 1/s)/2 - (s - 1/t)/2 = (s - 1/s - s + 1/t)/2 = ( -1/s + 1/t )/2 = (1/t - 1/s)/2.
Y = (1 - s/t)/2 = (t - s)/(2t)? Actually 1 - s/t = (t - s)/t, so Y = (t - s)/(2t). But note that t - s is negative (since t<s), so Y negative. So:

X - m = (s - t)/2 = -(t - s)/2.
n - X = (1/t - 1/s)/2 = (s - t)/(2st) = -(t - s)/(2st).
Y = (t - s)/(2t).

Thus (X - m)(n - X) = [ -(t - s)/2 ] * [ -(t - s)/(2st) ] = (t - s)^2/(4st). Then h_y = (t - s)^2/(4st) / Y = (t - s)^2/(4st) * (2t)/(t - s) = (t - s)^2 * 2t / (4st (t - s)) = (t - s)*2t/(4st) = (t - s)/(2s). So h_y = (t - s)/(2s). Since t < s, h_y is negative. Good.

Thus H = (X, h_y) = ( (s - 1/t)/2 , (t - s)/(2s) ).

Now, the line through H parallel to AP: direction d = (X, - (1+s/t)/2). So param: (x,y) = (X, (t-s)/(2s)) + u (X, - (1+s/t)/2).

We need to show that this line is tangent to circle (BEF). So we need to compute the distance from the center O of circle (BEF) to this line, and show that it equals the radius.

Alternatively, we can show that the line L is the polar of some point with respect to the circle (BEF). For a circle, the polar of a point on the circle is the tangent at that point. So if we can show that H is the pole of line AP (or something), then the line through H parallel to AP might be the polar of something else. But we have L parallel to AP, not necessarily AP itself.

Another idea: Since H is orthocenter of triangle PMN, we know that the reflection of H across MN lies on circumcircle of PMN. Also, the line through H parallel to AP might be related to the line through the reflection of something.

Let's compute the circumcenter of triangle PMN. Points: P, M(0,0), N(n,0) with n = (s-1/s)/2. So base MN on x-axis. The circumcenter O_PMN is on the perpendicular bisector of MN, which is vertical line x = n/2. Also, O_PMN is equidistant from P and M. So we can compute O_PMN = (n/2, y0). Then the orthocenter H = P+M+N - 2O_PMN = (X + 0 + n, Y + 0 + 0) - 2(n/2, y0) = (X+n, Y) - (n, 2y0) = (X, Y-2y0). So H = (X, Y-2y0). And we already have H = (X, (t-s)/(2s)). So Y-2y0 = (t-s)/(2s). We could compute y0, but maybe not needed.

Now, the line through H parallel to AP: direction v = (X, - (1+s/t)/2). So its equation: (x,y) = (X, (t-s)/(2s)) + u (X, - (1+s/t)/2).

We can also consider the line through A (0,1) with direction v: AP. That line: (x,y) = (0,1) + u (X, - (1+s/t)/2). So A, P, E, F lie on this line.

Now, the circle (BEF): B = (0,-1). E and F are on line AP. So we have a circle passing through B and two points on line AP. There is a known fact: For a given circle through B, the chord through A on line AP is such that the power of A with respect to that circle is AB *? Actually, A is on line AP, and E and F are the intersections of that line with the circle (BEF). So the power of A with respect to circle (BEF) is A E * A F? But careful: The line AP intersects circle (BEF) at E and F (since E and F are on that line and on the circle? Actually we defined E and F as intersections of line AP with Ω and Γ, respectively, not necessarily with circle (BEF). Wait, we need to verify: Circle (BEF) is the circumcircle of triangle BEF. So points B, E, F are on that circle. So line AP passes through E and F (since E and F are on AP). So line AP is a chord of circle (BEF) (through E and F). Thus A is a point on that line, but not necessarily on the circle. So the power of A relative to circle (BEF) is (AE)*(AF). That is interesting.

Now, we also have that A lies on both Ω and Γ. So we might compute AE and AF in terms of geometry. Since A, C, D are on Ω and Γ, and P is circumcenter of ACD, perhaps there is a relation: AP is the radical axis of circles (ACD) and something else? Actually, we can compute the power of A with respect to circle (ACD): Since A is on that circle, the power is 0. So A lies on that circle. And P is its center. So AP is a radius. Not helpful.

Now, consider the circle (BEF). Its center O is something. The line through H parallel to AP: we want it to be tangent. That line is essentially the line through H with direction AP. So if we translate the circle (BEF) by vector H - A, then the line AP becomes a line through H parallel to AP, which is the same as the translate of AP. So we can consider the translation T that sends A to H. Then T maps the circle (BEF) to some circle (B'E'F')? Actually, translation sends points: B -> B + (H-A) = (0,-1)+(X, (t-s)/(2s)-1). That might be messy. But note that the line through H parallel to AP is the image of AP under translation by vector H-A. So if we apply that translation to the whole configuration, the circle (BEF) will map to some circle that is tangent to the image of AP? Not.

Alternatively, we can consider the homothety with center H that sends line AP to a line through H? Actually, the line through H parallel to AP is just the line through H with direction AP; it's not a homothety of AP (since H is not necessarily on AP). So it's a translation.

Given the complexity, maybe we can find that the distance from O to L equals the distance from O to the line through H parallel to AP. And we can compute O in terms of t,s and then simplify.

Let's attempt to compute O directly using coordinates. Since B=(0,-1), we can find the circumcenter O by solving the system: |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 = |O-F|^2.

Let O = (a,b). Then:

(1) a^2 + (b+1)^2 = (a - λX)^2 + (b - (1-λw))^2? Actually E = (λX, 1-λw). So (a - λX)^2 + (b - (1-λw))^2 = a^2 + (b+1)^2.
(2) a^2 + (b+1)^2 = (a - μX)^2 + (b - (1-μw))^2.

Subtract (1) from (2) to get relation between λ and μ. But we can also solve for a and b directly.

Set up equations:

From (1): a^2 + (b+1)^2 = (a-λX)^2 + (b-1+λw)^2 = a^2 -2aλX + λ^2 X^2 + b^2 -2b(1-λw) + (1-λw)^2? Actually (b - (1-λw))^2 = (b-1+λw)^2 = b^2 + (1-λw)^2 -2b(1-λw). So expand:

a^2 + (b+1)^2 = a^2 -2aλX + λ^2 X^2 + b^2 + (1-λw)^2 -2b(1-λw).

Cancel a^2 and b^2 on both sides? Actually left has a^2 + (b+1)^2 = a^2 + b^2 + 2b +1. Right has a^2 + b^2 + λ^2 X^2 + (1-λw)^2 -2aλX -2b(1-λw). So:

a^2+b^2+2b+1 = a^2+b^2+ λ^2 X^2 + (1-λw)^2 -2aλX -2b(1-λw)

Cancel a^2+b^2:

2b+1 = λ^2 X^2 + (1-λw)^2 -2aλX -2b(1-λw).

Thus:

2b+1 = λ^2 X^2 + (1-λw)^2 -2aλX -2b(1-λw).

Bring terms:

2b+1 +2b(1-λw) = λ^2 X^2 + (1-λw)^2 -2aλX.

Left: 2b+1+2b -2bλw = 4b -2bλw +1 = 2b(2-λw)+1.

So:

2b(2-λw)+1 = λ^2 X^2 + (1-λw)^2 -2aλX.   (Equation A)

Similarly, from (2) with μ:

2b(2-μw)+1 = μ^2 X^2 + (1-μw)^2 -2aμX.   (Equation B)

Subtract (A) from (B):

2b(2-μw) - 2b(2-λw) = (μ^2 X^2 + (1-μw)^2 -2aμX) - (λ^2 X^2 + (1-λw)^2 -2aλX).

Left: 2b[(2-μw)-(2-λw)] = 2b[ -μw+λw] = 2b w (λ-μ).

Right: μ^2 X^2 - λ^2 X^2 + (1-μw)^2 - (1-λw)^2 -2aX(μ-λ) = X^2(μ^2-λ^2) + [ (1-μw)^2 - (1-λw)^2 ] + 2aX(λ-μ). Because -2aX(μ-λ) = 2aX(λ-μ). Now, (1-μw)^2 - (1-λw)^2 = (1-μw -1+λw)(1-μw+1-λw) = w(λ-μ)(2 - (λ+μ)w). So right side = X^2(μ-λ)(μ+λ) + w(λ-μ)(2 - (λ+μ)w) + 2aX(λ-μ) = (λ-μ)[ -X^2(λ+μ) + w(2 - (λ+μ)w) + 2aX ]? Wait, factor (λ-μ): μ^2-λ^2 = (μ-λ)(μ+λ) = -(λ-μ)(λ+μ). So X^2(μ^2-λ^2) = -X^2(λ-μ)(λ+μ). Then plus w(λ-μ)(2 - (λ+μ)w). So total = (λ-μ)[ -X^2(λ+μ) + w(2 - (λ+μ)w) ] + 2aX(λ-μ). So right side = (λ-μ)[ -X^2(λ+μ) + w(2 - (λ+μ)w) + 2aX ].

Thus we have:

2b w (λ-μ) = (λ-μ)[ -X^2(λ+μ) + w(2 - (λ+μ)w) + 2aX ].

Since λ ≠ μ (E and F distinct), we can divide by (λ-μ):

2b w = -X^2(λ+μ) + w(2 - (λ+μ)w) + 2aX.

Thus:

2b w = -X^2(λ+μ) + 2w - w^2(λ+μ) + 2aX.

Rearrange:

2b w = 2w - (λ+μ)(X^2 + w^2) + 2aX.

So:

2b w = 2w - (λ+μ)K + 2aX, where K = X^2+w^2.

Thus:

2b w - 2w = - (λ+μ)K + 2aX => 2w(b-1) = - (λ+μ)K + 2aX.   (Equation C)

Now, we also have Equation A: 2b(2-λw)+1 = λ^2 X^2 + (1-λw)^2 -2aλX.

Let's simplify the right side: λ^2 X^2 + (1-λw)^2 -2aλX = λ^2 X^2 + 1 -2λw + λ^2 w^2 -2aλX = λ^2 (X^2+w^2) + 1 -2λw -2aλX = λ^2 K + 1 -2λw -2aλX.

Thus Equation A becomes:

2b(2-λw)+1 = λ^2 K + 1 -2λw -2aλX => cancel 1:

2b(2-λw) = λ^2 K -2λw -2aλX.

Expand left: 4b -2bλw = λ^2 K -2λw -2aλX.

Bring terms: 4b -2bλw +2λw = λ^2 K -2aλX => 4b + 2λw(1 - b) = λ^2 K -2aλX.

Thus:

4b + 2λw(1-b) = λ^2 K -2aλX.   (Equation A')

Similarly, for μ: 4b + 2μw(1-b) = μ^2 K -2aμX.   (Equation B')

Now, we have two equations (A') and (B') in a,b. Also, we have relation (C): 2w(b-1) = - (λ+μ)K + 2aX.

We can try to solve for a and b. Since we have only two unknowns, we can eliminate a or b.

From (A'): 4b + 2λw(1-b) = λ^2 K -2aλX => 4b + 2λw -2λw b = λ^2 K -2aλX => 4b -2λw b = λ^2 K -2λw -2aλX => b(4 - 2λw) = λ^2 K -2λw -2aλX => b(2 - λw) = (λ^2 K -2λw -2aλX)/2? Actually divide both sides by 2: b(2-λw) = (λ^2 K -2λw -2aλX)/2? Let's not.

Alternatively, solve for a from (A'): 2aλX = λ^2 K -4b -2λw(1-b) => a = [λ^2 K -4b -2λw(1-b)]/(2λX). Provided λ≠0. Since λ = s_E >0, ok.

Similarly from (B'): a = [μ^2 K -4b -2μw(1-b)]/(2μX).

Set them equal:

[λ^2 K -4b -2λw(1-b)]/(2λX) = [μ^2 K -4b -2μw(1-b)]/(2μX) => Multiply both sides by 2X: (λ^2 K -4b -2λw(1-b))/λ = (μ^2 K -4b -2μw(1-b))/μ => Multiply both sides by λμ: μ(λ^2 K -4b -2λw(1-b)) = λ(μ^2 K -4b -2μw(1-b)) => μλ^2 K -4μb -2μλw(1-b) = λμ^2 K -4λb -2λμw(1-b) => Cancel -2μλw(1-b) = -2λμw(1-b) (same), so they cancel. Then we have:

μλ^2 K -4μb = λμ^2 K -4λb => Divide by μ? Actually bring terms: μλ^2 K - λμ^2 K = 4μb -4λb => λμ(λ - μ)K = 4b(μ-λ) => λμ(λ-μ)K = -4b(λ-μ) => Since λ≠μ, divide by (λ-μ): λμ K = -4b => b = - (λμ K)/4.

Thus we have b expressed in terms of λ, μ, K.

Now, from (C): 2w(b-1) = - (λ+μ)K + 2aX => b-1 = [ - (λ+μ)K + 2aX ]/(2w). So aX = w(b-1)/2 + (λ+μ)K/2? Actually rearr: 2aX = 2w(b-1) + (λ+μ)K => aX = w(b-1) + (λ+μ)K/2.

But we also have a from earlier expression. Since we have b, we can compute a.

Let's compute b first.

b = - (λμ K)/4.

Now, recall λ = s_E = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2). μ = s_F = 2( t^2 + st + s^2 t^2 + t/s ) / ((1+t^2)(1+s^2)). But we might find a simpler expression for λμ and K in terms of s,t.

We have:

X = (s - 1/t)/2.
w = (1 + s/t)/2.
K = X^2 + w^2 = (1/4)[ (s - 1/t)^2 + (1 + s/t)^2 ] = (1/4)[ s^2 - 2s/t + 1/t^2 + 1 + 2s/t + s^2/t^2 ] = (1/4)[ s^2 + 1/t^2 + 1 + s^2/t^2 ] = (1/4)(1 + s^2 + 1/t^2 + s^2/t^2) = (1/4)(1 + s^2)(1 + 1/t^2) = (1/4)(1+s^2)(1+1/t^2). So K = (1+s^2)(1+1/t^2)/4.

Now, λ = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2). So λ = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2). Then λμ K =? We'll compute b = - (λμ K)/4 = - (λμ * (1+s^2)(1+1/t^2)/4)/4 = - (λμ (1+s^2)(1+1/t^2))/16. But λ contains (1+s^2) in denominator, so λ(1+s^2) = 2(1+st). So λμ K = μ * 2(1+st) * (1+1/t^2)/4? Actually:

λμ K = μ * λ * K = μ * (2(1+st)/(1+s^2)) * ((1+s^2)(1+1/t^2)/4) = μ * 2(1+st) * (1+1/t^2)/4 = μ * (1+st)(1+1/t^2)/2.

Thus b = - (μ (1+st)(1+1/t^2)/2)/4 = - μ (1+st)(1+1/t^2)/8.

So b = - μ (1+st)(1+1/t^2)/8.

Now, we also have expression for b from earlier? That's fine.

Now, we need to compute a. From aX = w(b-1) + (λ+μ)K/2. We can compute a in terms of t,s. Let's compute each term.

First, compute w = (1+s/t)/2 = (t+s)/(2t). Because 1 + s/t = (t+s)/t, so w = (t+s)/(2t). Similarly, X = (s - 1/t)/2 = (st - 1)/(2t). So X = (st-1)/(2t). Also, K = (1+s^2)(1+1/t^2)/4 = ((1+s^2)(t^2+1))/(4t^2). So K = (1+s^2)(1+t^2)/(4t^2). Note: (1+1/t^2) = (t^2+1)/t^2.

Now, compute λ+μ. λ = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2). μ = 2( t^2 + st + s^2 t^2 + t/s ) / ((1+t^2)(1+s^2)). Let's simplify μ. Write μ = 2( t^2 + st + s^2 t^2 + t/s ) / ((1+t^2)(1+s^2)). Factor t? So μ = 2 t(1+s^2)(t + 1/s) / ((1+t^2)(1+s^2)) = 2 t (t + 1/s) / (1+t^2) = 2 t (t + 1/s) / (1+t^2). So μ = 2t (t + 1/s) / (1+t^2). That is a nice simplification! Let's check: μ = 2t(t+1/s)/(1+t^2). For u=0,t=1,s=2.718, then μ = 2*1*(1+0.3679)/2 = 2*1.3679/2 = 1.3679, which matches s_F ≈1.369. Good.

Thus we have:

λ = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2)
μ = 2t(t+1/s)/(1+t^2) = 2t(t+1/s)/(1+t^2)

Now, we also have λμ = λ * μ = [2(1+st)/(1+s^2)] * [2t(t+1/s)/(1+t^2)] = 4t (1+st)(t+1/s) / ((1+s^2)(1+t^2)).

Now, compute b = - μ (1+st)(1+1/t^2)/8. But note: (1+1/t^2) = (t^2+1)/t^2. So b = - μ (1+st)(t^2+1)/(8t^2). And μ = 2t(t+1/s)/(1+t^2). So b = - [2t(t+1/s)/(1+t^2)] * (1+st)(t^2+1)/(8t^2) = - (2t(t+1/s)(1+st)(t^2+1)) / ((1+t^2) * 8 t^2) = - (t+1/s)(1+st) / (4t). Because 2t/(8t^2)=1/(4t), and (t^2+1)/(1+t^2)=1. So b = - (t+1/s)(1+st) / (4t). Good.

Thus b = - (t+1/s)(1+st) / (4t).

Now, we also have h_y = (t-s)/(2s). And we want to show that the line L through H = (X, h_y) with direction d = (X, -w) (since direction vector d = (X, -w) where w = (1+s/t)/2). So L: (x,y) = (X, h_y) + u (X, -w). The distance from O = (a,b) to L must equal radius R_c. The radius R_c = distance from O to B (or E,F). Since B=(0,-1), we can compute R_c^2 = a^2 + (b+1)^2.

Now, we need to compute a. From earlier, aX = w(b-1) + (λ+μ)K/2. We can compute a directly.

Compute (λ+μ)K/2. First, K = (1+s^2)(1+t^2)/(4t^2). Then (λ+μ)K/2 = (λ+μ) * (1+s^2)(1+t^2)/(8t^2). Also, w(b-1) = w(b-1). Compute b-1 = - (t+1/s)(1+st)/(4t) - 1 = - (t+1/s)(1+st)/(4t) - 1. We can combine.

But maybe we can find a simpler expression for a. Since we have b, and we know that O is the circumcenter, we could compute a by using the fact that O lies on the perpendicular bisector of BE or BF, and we have a relation from the equation for a from (A')? Actually we already used that to get b. We can compute a from the equation aX = w(b-1) + (λ+μ)K/2. Since we have X and w in terms of t,s, we can compute a. But we also have that a should be something like? Possibly a = X? Let's test numeric: t=1, s=2.718, X=0.859, w= (1+2.718)/2=1.859, b = - (t+1/s)(1+st)/(4t) = - (1+0.3679)*(1+2.718)/(4) = - (1.3679)*(3.718)/4 = - (5.090)/4 = -1.2725. Then b-1 = -2.2725. w(b-1)=1.859*(-2.2725)= -4.222. Then aX = -4.222+4.7275=0.5055, X=0.859, so a=0.5055/0.859=0.588. That matches earlier O ≈ (0.586, -1.272). So a is not X. So a is something else.

Now, we need to compute the distance from O to L. L passes through H = (X, h_y) with direction d = (X, -w). The distance from O to L is given by |(O - H) × d| / |d|. Let’s compute O-H = (a - X, b - h_y). We have a, X, b, h_y in terms of t,s. We want to show that this distance equals R_c = sqrt(a^2+(b+1)^2).

So we need to verify:

|(a - X, b - h_y) × (X, -w)| / sqrt(X^2+w^2) = sqrt(a^2+(b+1)^2).

Cross product (2D) = (a-X)*(-w) - (b-h_y)*X = -(a-X)w - X(b-h_y) = -(a-X)w - X(b-h_y). So absolute value = |(a-X)w + X(b-h_y)|. So condition:

| (a-X)w + X(b-h_y) | / sqrt(K) = sqrt(a^2+(b+1)^2).

Square both sides:

[ (a-X)w + X(b-h_y) ]^2 / K = a^2+(b+1)^2.

Thus we need to show:

[ (a-X)w + X(b-h_y) ]^2 = K (a^2+(b+1)^2).   (Equation D)

Now, we have explicit expressions for a, X, w, b, h_y in terms of t,s. We can try to compute both sides and simplify.

First, compute X and w:

X = (st - 1)/(2t)
w = (t + s)/(2t)

So note: X = (st-1)/(2t), w = (t+s)/(2t). Also, K = X^2+w^2 = (1/4t^2)[ (st-1)^2 + (t+s)^2 ] = (1/4t^2)[ s^2 t^2 -2st+1 + t^2+2st+s^2 ] = (1/4t^2)[ s^2 t^2 + t^2 + s^2 + 1 ] = (1/4t^2)[ (1+s^2)(1+t^2) ]. So K = (1+s^2)(1+t^2)/(4t^2). Good.

Now, compute b = - (t+1/s)(1+st)/(4t). We can also write b in terms of s,t.

We have h_y = (t-s)/(2s). So b - h_y = - (t+1/s)(1+st)/(4t) - (t-s)/(2s). Let's compute b - h_y.

b = - (t+1/s)(1+st)/(4t). Expand numerator: (t+1/s)(1+st) = t(1+st) + (1/s)(1+st) = t + s t^2 + 1/s + t = 2t + s t^2 + 1/s. So b = - (2t + s t^2 + 1/s)/(4t). Then b - h_y = - (2t + s t^2 + 1/s)/(4t) - (t-s)/(2s) = - (2t + s t^2 + 1/s)/(4t) - (t-s)/(2s). Put over common denominator: 4t s? Actually compute:

b - h_y = - [ (2t + s t^2 + 1/s) * s + (t-s) * 2t ] / (4t s) = - [ (2t s + s^2 t^2 + 1) + (2t^2 - 2st) ] / (4t s) = - [ 2t s + s^2 t^2 + 1 + 2t^2 - 2st ] / (4t s) = - [ s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1 ] / (4t s). Because 2t s - 2st = 0. So b - h_y = - (s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1)/(4t s). Note that s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1 = t^2(s^2+2) + 1? Not needed.

Given that we have numeric verification, we can attempt to prove the identity by substituting the expressions and simplifying. This is doable with careful algebra. Since we have expressions for a in terms of t,s, and we have b, X, w, h_y, we can compute both sides of Equation D.

Thus a = (t(1+s^2) / (2A(st-1))) * (λ^2 K - 4b - 2λw(1-b)). So a is expressed in terms of t,s.

Set:

Alternatively, we can compute a using the formula for the circumcenter of three points. We already used that to get b and then a. Since we have b, we can compute a by solving the equation for a from (A') or (B'). Let's use (A'):

4b + 2λw(1-b) = λ^2 K - 2aλX.

So 2aλX = λ^2 K - 4b - 2λw(1-b).

We already computed λ^2 K = (1+st)^2 (1+t^2)/(t^2 (1+s^2)). And we have 4b = - (t+1/s)(1+st)/t. So -4b = (t+1/s)(1+st)/t.

Thus λ^2 K - 4b = λ^2 K + (t+1/s)(1+st)/t. Let's denote A = 1+st. Then λ^2 K = A^2 (1+t^2)/(t^2 (1+s^2)). And (t+1/s)A/t = A(t+1/s)/t.

Now, compute 2λw(1-b). We have w = (t+s)/(2t). So 2λw = 2 * [2A/(1+s^2)] * (t+s)/(2t) = 2A(t+s)/(t(1+s^2)). Then 2λw(1-b) = 2A(t+s)/(t(1+s^2)) * (1-b). And 1-b = (s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1)/(4ts) as before. So 2λw(1-b) = 2A(t+s)/(t(1+s^2)) * (s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1)/(4ts) = A(t+s)(s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1) / (2 t^2 s (1+s^2)).

Thus λ^2 K - 4b - 2λw(1-b) = A^2 (1+t^2)/(t^2 (1+s^2)) + A(t+1/s)/t - A(t+s)(s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1) / (2 t^2 s (1+s^2)).

Factor A/(t^2 (1+s^2))? Let's write everything over common denominator t^2 s (1+s^2). We have:

A^2 (1+t^2)/(t^2 (1+s^2)) = A^2 (1+t^2) * s / (t^2 s (1+s^2)).

A(t+1/s)/t = A(t+1/s)/t = A(t+1/s) * t s / (t^2 s) = A(t s + 1) * t s? Wait: A(t+1/s)/t = A (t+1/s)/t = A ( (t s + 1)/s )/t = A (t s + 1)/(s t). Multiply numerator and denominator by t s: Actually to get denominator t^2 s (1+s^2), we write A(t+1/s)/t = A(t+1/s) * t s / (t^2 s) = A(t s + 1) * t s? No: (t+1/s) = (t s + 1)/s. Term1: A^2 (1+t^2) s / (t^2 s (1+s^2)) = A^2 s (1+t^2) / (t^2 s (1+s^2)) = A^2 (1+t^2) / (t^2 (1+s^2))? Actually careful: The denominator we want is t^2 s (1+s^2). So term1 = A^2 (1+t^2) / (t^2 (1+s^2)) = A^2 (1+t^2) * s / (t^2 s (1+s^2)). So term1 = A^2 s (1+t^2) / (t^2 s (1+s^2)).

Term2: A(t s + 1)/(s t) = A(t s + 1) * t (1+s^2) / (s t * t (1+s^2)) = A t (t s + 1)(1+s^2) / (t^2 s (1+s^2)). So term2 = A t (t s + 1)(1+s^2) / (t^2 s (1+s^2)).

Thus λ^2 K - 4b - 2λw(1-b) = [A^2 s (1+t^2) + A t (t s + 1)(1+s^2) - A (t+s)(s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1)/2] / (t^2 s (1+s^2)). So:

Now, recall that a = (λ^2 K - 4b - 2λw(1-b))/(2λX). And 2λX = 2 * [2A/(1+s^2)] * X = 4A X/(1+s^2). And X = (st-1)/(2t). So 2λX = 4A * (st-1)/(2t) / (1+s^2) = 2A(st-1)/(t(1+s^2)). Thus:

a = [λ^2 K - 4b - 2λw(1-b)] * [t(1+s^2)] / [2A(st-1)].

Substitute the numerator expression:

a = [A / (t^2 s (1+s^2)) * ( bracket )] * [t(1+s^2)] / [2A(st-1)] = [A * bracket * t(1+s^2)] / [t^2 s (1+s^2) * 2A(st-1)] = [bracket] / [2 t s (st-1)].

So a = [A s (1+t^2) + t (t s + 1)(1+s^2) - (t+s)(s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1)/2] / (2 t s (st-1)). And recall A = 1+st.

Thus a = [ (1+st) s (1+t^2) + t (ts+1)(1+s^2) - (t+s)(s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1)/2 ] / (2 t s (st-1)).

Now, we need to compute (a-X)w + X(b-h_y). This expression might simplify nicely.

First, compute X = (st-1)/(2t). w = (t+s)/(2t). So we have:

a - X = a - (st-1)/(2t).

Now, compute (a-X)w = w a - w X. But w X = (t+s)/(2t) * (st-1)/(2t) = (t+s)(st-1)/(4t^2). So:

(a-X)w = a w - (t+s)(st-1)/(4t^2).

Then (a-X)w + X(b-h_y) = a w - (t+s)(st-1)/(4t^2) + X(b-h_y).

Now, X(b-h_y) = (st-1)/(2t) * (b - h_y). We have b - h_y = - (s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1)/(4t s). So X(b-h_y) = (st-1)/(2t) * [ - (s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1)/(4t s) ] = - (st-1)(s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1)/(8 t^2 s).

Thus the left side LHS = a w - (t+s)(st-1)/(4t^2) - (st-1)(s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1)/(8 t^2 s).

Factor (st-1)/(t^2)? Actually a w is unknown. But we have a expression for a. So we can compute a w.

a = [ bracket ] / (2 t s (st-1)). So a w = [ bracket ] / (2 t s (st-1)) * (t+s)/(2t) = [ bracket ] * (t+s) / (4 t^2 s (st-1)). So:

a w = (t+s) * bracket / (4 t^2 s (st-1)).

Thus LHS = (t+s) * bracket / (4 t^2 s (st-1)) - (t+s)(st-1)/(4t^2) - (st-1)(s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1)/(8 t^2 s).

Now, note that bracket = A s (1+t^2) + t (ts+1)(1+s^2) - (t+s)(s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1)/2. Let's compute bracket explicitly in terms of s,t. Perhaps we can factor (st-1) out? Let's attempt to simplify bracket.

Let’s denote:

B1 = (1+st) s (1+t^2) = s (1+st)(1+t^2).
B2 = t (ts+1)(1+s^2) = t (1+st)(1+s^2).
B3 = (t+s)(s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1)/2.

So bracket = (1+st)[ s(1+t^2) + t(1+s^2) ] - (t+s)(s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1)/2.

Now, compute s(1+t^2) + t(1+s^2) = s + s t^2 + t + t s^2 = (s+t) + st(s+t) = (s+t)(1+st). So B1+B2 = (1+st) * (s+t)(1+st) = (1+st)^2 (s+t). So bracket = (1+st)^2 (s+t) - (t+s)(s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1)/2 = (s+t)[ (1+st)^2 - (s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1)/2 ].

Thus bracket = (s+t) * [ (1+st)^2 - (s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1)/2 ].

Now, compute (1+st)^2 = 1 + 2st + s^2 t^2. So:

(1+st)^2 - (s^2 t^2 + 6ts + 1)/2 = (1 + 2st + s^2 t^2) - (1/2 + 3st + s^2 t^2/2) = (1 - 1/2) + (2st - 3st) + (s^2 t^2 - s^2 t^2/2) = 1/2 - st + (s^2 t^2)/2.

So bracket = (s+t) * (1/2 - st + (s^2 t^2)/2) = (s+t) * ( (1 - 2st + s^2 t^2)/2 ) = (s+t) * ( (1 - st)^2 / 2 ). Because (1 - st)^2 = 1 - 2st + s^2 t^2.

Thus bracket = (s+t) * (1 - st)^2 / 2.

That is a beautiful simplification! So bracket = (s+t)(1-st)^2/2.

Now, recall that a = bracket / (2 t s (st-1)). But note that (1-st)^2 = (st-1)^2, so bracket = (s+t)(st-1)^2/2. Then:

a = [ (s+t)(st-1)^2/2 ] / (2 t s (st-1)) = (s+t)(st-1)^2 / (4 t s (st-1)) = (s+t)(st-1) / (4 t s).

Thus a = (s+t)(st-1) / (4 t s). That is a nice simple expression.

Now, we can compute a - X: X = (st-1)/(2t). So a - X = (s+t)(st-1)/(4 t s) - (st-1)/(2t) = (st-1)/(4 t s) * [ (s+t) - 2s ]? Because (st-1)/(2t) = (st-1)/(2t) * (2s)/(2s) = (st-1)*2s/(4 t s). So:

a - X = (st-1)/(4 t s) * [ (s+t) - 2s ] = (st-1)/(4 t s) * (t - s). So a - X = (st-1)(t-s)/(4 t s). Note that t-s is negative (since t<s). So a-X negative.

Now, we have w = (t+s)/(2t). And we have b - h_y = - (s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1)/(4 t s). Let's also compute b+1 = - (st-1)^2/(4 t s). That we already have.

Now, compute (a-X)w + X(b-h_y) = (st-1)(t-s)/(4 t s) * (t+s)/(2t) + (st-1)/(2t) * [ - (s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1)/(4 t s) ] = (st-1)(t-s)(t+s)/(8 t^2 s) - (st-1)(s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1)/(8 t^2 s) = (st-1)/(8 t^2 s) * [ (t-s)(t+s) - (s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1) ].

Now, (t-s)(t+s) = t^2 - s^2. So:

= (st-1)/(8 t^2 s) * [ t^2 - s^2 - s^2 t^2 - 2t^2 - 1 ] = (st-1)/(8 t^2 s) * [ t^2 - s^2 - s^2 t^2 - 2t^2 - 1 ] = (st-1)/(8 t^2 s) * [ - s^2 - s^2 t^2 - t^2 - 1 ]? Because t^2 - 2t^2 = -t^2. So inside: -t^2 - s^2 - s^2 t^2 - 1 = -(t^2 + s^2 + s^2 t^2 + 1). So:

(a-X)w + X(b-h_y) = - (st-1)/(8 t^2 s) * (t^2 + s^2 + s^2 t^2 + 1) = - (st-1)/(8 t^2 s) * (1 + t^2)(1 + s^2). Because (1+t^2)(1+s^2) = 1 + t^2 + s^2 + s^2 t^2.

Thus:

(a-X)w + X(b-h_y) = - (st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2) / (8 t^2 s).

Now, we need to compute the right side of Equation D: K (a^2+(b+1)^2). We have K = (1+s^2)(1+t^2)/(4t^2). And a^2+(b+1)^2 = a^2 + (st-1)^4/(16 t^2 s^2). Let's compute a^2.

a = (s+t)(st-1)/(4 t s). So a^2 = (s+t)^2 (st-1)^2 / (16 t^2 s^2).

Thus a^2+(b+1)^2 = (st-1)^2/(16 t^2 s^2) * [ (s+t)^2 + (st-1)^2 ]? Because b+1 = - (st-1)^2/(4 t s), so (b+1)^2 = (st-1)^4/(16 t^2 s^2). So a^2+(b+1)^2 = (st-1)^2/(16 t^2 s^2) * [ (s+t)^2 + (st-1)^2 ]? Wait: a^2 = (s+t)^2 (st-1)^2/(16 t^2 s^2). So a^2+(b+1)^2 = (st-1)^2/(16 t^2 s^2) * [ (s+t)^2 + (st-1)^2 ]. Because (b+1)^2 = (st-1)^4/(16 t^2 s^2) = (st-1)^2 * (st-1)^2/(16 t^2 s^2). So indeed:

a^2+(b+1)^2 = (st-1)^2/(16 t^2 s^2) * [ (s+t)^2 + (st-1)^2 ].

Now, compute (s+t)^2 + (st-1)^2 = s^2+2st+t^2 + s^2 t^2 -2st+1 = s^2+t^2+ s^2 t^2+1 = (1+s^2)(1+t^2). So:

a^2+(b+1)^2 = (st-1)^2/(16 t^2 s^2) * (1+s^2)(1+t^2).

Thus K (a^2+(b+1)^2) = [ (1+s^2)(1+t^2)/(4t^2) ] * [ (st-1)^2/(16 t^2 s^2) * (1+s^2)(1+t^2) ] = (st-1)^2 (1+s^2)^2 (1+t^2)^2 / (64 t^4 s^2).

Now, the left side squared: [ (a-X)w + X(b-h_y) ]^2 = [ - (st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2) / (8 t^2 s) ]^2 = (st-1)^2 (1+t^2)^2 (1+s^2)^2 / (64 t^4 s^2).

So indeed, the left side squared equals the right side squared. Since both sides are positive (we consider absolute values), we have:

[ (a-X)w + X(b-h_y) ]^2 = K (a^2+(b+1)^2).

Thus |(a-X)w + X(b-h_y)| / sqrt(K) = sqrt(a^2+(b+1)^2). That is exactly the distance from O to L equals the radius R_c (since sqrt(a^2+(b+1)^2) = distance from O to B = R_c). So the line L is tangent to circle (BEF). This completes the proof.

Thus we have a rigorous coordinate proof.

1. Set up coordinate system: Let the common chord AB be the y-axis, and the line of centers MN be the x-axis. By scaling, we may assume A=(0,1), B=(0,-1). Let M=(m,0), N=(n,0) with m<n (since C, M, N, D in that order). Then the circles are:
   Ω: (x-m)^2+y^2 = m^2+1,
   Γ: (x-n)^2+y^2 = n^2+1.

2. Introduce hyperbolic parameters: Let m = sinh u, n = sinh v. Then u<v because sinh is increasing. Let t = e^u, s = e^v. Then t>0, s>0, and t<s because u<v. Then we have:
   r = cosh u = (t+1/t)/2, R = cosh v = (s+1/s)/2.
   The points C and D: C = (m - r, 0) = (-e^{-u}, 0) = (-1/t, 0), D = (n + R, 0) = (e^v, 0) = (s, 0).

3. Compute P, the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since A=(0,1), C=(-1/t,0), D=(s,0). The perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line x = (s - 1/t)/2. So P has x-coordinate X = (s - 1/t)/2. Let P=(X, Y). Then Y is determined by PA=PC. Compute: PC^2 = (X+1/t)^2+Y^2, PA^2 = X^2+(Y-1)^2. Setting equal and using the fact that X = (s-1/t)/2, we find Y = (1 - s/t)/2. So P=(X, Y). Also note that Y-1 = -(1+s/t)/2.

4. Define line AP: direction vector v = (X, Y-1) = (X, -w) where w = (1+s/t)/2. So v = (X, -w). Also, note that X = (st-1)/(2t), w = (t+s)/(2t). So v = ((st-1)/(2t), -(t+s)/(2t)). 

5. Find E and F: E is second intersection of line AP with Ω. Parameterize AP: (0,1) + λ v. Substitute into Ω: (x-m)^2+y^2 = m^2+1. Using m = sinh u = (t-1/t)/2, we compute. After simplification, we get λ = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2) = λ_E. So E = (λ_E X, 1 - λ_E w). Similarly, F corresponds to λ_F = 2t(t+1/s)/(1+t^2). So F = (λ_F X, 1 - λ_F w). 

6. Compute the circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Let B=(0,-1). We set up equations |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 = |O-F|^2. Write O=(a,b). Using the condition |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2, we derive after simplification:
   b = - (t+1/s)(1+st)/(4t) = - (1+st)(t+1/s)/(4t). Also, from the condition |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2, we find that a = (s+t)(st-1)/(4ts). (We can present the derivation by solving the linear system, using the fact that λ_E and λ_F are as above and simplifying using algebra; we can show that the resulting a and b are these expressions.)

7. Compute H, the orthocenter of triangle PMN. Points: P=(X,Y), M=(m,0)=( (t-1/t)/2, 0), N=(n,0)=( (s-1/s)/2, 0). Since M and N lie on x-axis, the altitude from P to MN is vertical line x=X. So H lies on x=X. Also, the altitude from M to PN: line through M perpendicular to PN. Compute PN vector: (n - X, -Y). Using coordinates, we find that the intersection of the vertical line x=X and the altitude from M yields H = (X, h_y) where h_y = (X-m)(n-X)/Y. Simplify using expressions for X,m,n,Y: X = (st-1)/(2t), m = (t-1/t)/2, n = (s-1/s)/2, Y = (1-st)/(2t). After simplification, we get h_y = (t-s)/(2s). So H = (X, (t-s)/(2s)). 

8. Consider the line L through H parallel to AP. Since AP has direction v = (X, -w), L is given by (x,y) = (X, h_y) + u (X, -w). We need to show that L is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF, which has center O=(a,b) and radius R_c = distance from O to B = sqrt(a^2+(b+1)^2). The distance from O to L is |(O-H) × v| / |v|. Compute O-H = (a-X, b-h_y). Compute the cross product (2D) = (a-X)*(-w) - (b-h_y)*X = -(a-X)w - X(b-h_y). Compute a-X and b-h_y using the expressions derived. We find:
   a-X = (st-1)(t-s)/(4ts) = (st-1)(t-s)/(4ts).
   b-h_y = - (t+1/s)(1+st)/(4t) - (t-s)/(2s) = - (s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1)/(4ts) (after simplification).
   Then (a-X)w + X(b-h_y) = ... (we computed earlier). Simplify to get = - (st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2)/(8 t^2 s). Then |(a-X)w + X(b-h_y)| = (st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2)/(8 t^2 s). And |v| = sqrt(X^2+w^2) = sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2)/(4t^2)) = (1/2) sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/t. So the distance from O to L is:
   d = [ (st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2)/(8 t^2 s) ] / [ (1/2) sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/t ] = (st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2)/(8 t^2 s) * (2 t)/ sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2)) = (st-1) sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/(4 s).
   Now, compute R_c = distance from O to B = sqrt(a^2+(b+1)^2). We have a = (s+t)(st-1)/(4ts), b+1 = - (st-1)^2/(4ts). So a^2+(b+1)^2 = (st-1)^2/(16 t^2 s^2) [ (s+t)^2 + (st-1)^2 ] = (st-1)^2/(16 t^2 s^2) * (1+s^2)(1+t^2). Thus R_c = (st-1) sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/(4 t s). Wait, that gives R_c = (st-1) sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/(4 t s). But our d = (st-1) sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/(4 s). These are not equal unless t=1? That suggests a mistake: I think I miscomputed the distance from O to L. Let's recompute the cross product magnitude correctly.

We have O-H = (a-X, b-h_y). v = (X, -w). The absolute value of the cross product (2D) is |(a-X)(-w) - (b-h_y)X| = |-(a-X)w - X(b-h_y)| = |(a-X)w + X(b-h_y)|. So the distance = |(a-X)w + X(b-h_y)| / |v|.

We computed (a-X)w + X(b-h_y) = - (st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2)/(8 t^2 s). So absolute value = (st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2)/(8 t^2 s). Good.

Now, |v| = sqrt(X^2+w^2). X = (st-1)/(2t), w = (t+s)/(2t). So X^2+w^2 = (1/(4t^2))[ (st-1)^2 + (t+s)^2 ] = (1/(4t^2))[ (s^2 t^2 -2st+1) + (t^2+2st+s^2) ] = (1/(4t^2))[ s^2 t^2 + t^2 + s^2 +1 ] = (1/(4t^2))(1+s^2)(1+t^2). So |v| = sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/(2t). Thus:

d = [ (st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2)/(8 t^2 s) ] / [ sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/(2t) ] = (st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2)/(8 t^2 s) * (2t)/ sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2)) = (st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2) * 2t / (8 t^2 s sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))) = (st-1) sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2)) / (4 t s). Because (1+t^2)(1+s^2)/ sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2)) = sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2)). And 2t/(8 t^2)=1/(4t). So d = (st-1) sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/(4 t s). So indeed d = (st-1) sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/(4 t s). Now, R_c = sqrt(a^2+(b+1)^2). We have a = (s+t)(st-1)/(4ts), b+1 = - (st-1)^2/(4ts). So a^2+(b+1)^2 = (st-1)^2/(16 t^2 s^2) [ (s+t)^2 + (st-1)^2 ] = (st-1)^2/(16 t^2 s^2) * (1+s^2)(1+t^2). Thus R_c = (st-1) sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/(4 t s). So d = R_c. Great! So the distance equals the radius. Hence L is tangent to circle (BEF).

Thus the proof is complete.

I'll structure:

- Setup coordinate system with AB as y-axis, MN as x-axis, and scale so that A=(0,1), B=(0,-1). Let M=(m,0), N=(n,0) with m<n.

- Introduce hyperbolic parameters: set m = sinh u, n = sinh v, u<v. Let t = e^u, s = e^v. Then t,s>0, t<s. Compute coordinates of C,D.

- Compute P, the circumcenter of ACD: solve using perpendicular bisectors. Obtain X = (s - 1/t)/2, Y = (1 - s/t)/2.

- Find E,F: param line AP, plug into circle equations, solve for λ. Obtain λ_E = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2), λ_F = 2t(t+1/s)/(1+t^2). So E = (λ_E X, 1 - λ_E w), F = (λ_F X, 1 - λ_F w), where w = (1+s/t)/2.

- Find O = circumcenter of BEF: Solve |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 and |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2. Derive expressions for a and b. Show that a = (s+t)(st-1)/(4ts) and b = -(1+st)(t+1/s)/(4t). (Provide derivation: subtract the two equations to get b = -λμK/4, then substitute and simplify.)

- Compute H: orthocenter of PMN. Since M,N on x-axis, altitude from P is vertical x=X. Altitude from M to PN: line through M perpendicular to PN. Intersection gives H = (X, (t-s)/(2s)). Simplify.

- Compute the line L through H parallel to AP: direction v = (X, -w). So L: (x,y) = (X, (t-s)/(2s)) + u (X, -w).

- Compute distance from O to L: d = |(O-H) × v| / |v|. Compute O-H = (a-X, b-h_y). Compute cross product: (a-X)(-w) - (b-h_y)X = -(a-X)w - X(b-h_y). Compute a-X and b-h_y using expressions: a-X = (st-1)(t-s)/(4ts), b-h_y = - (s^2 t^2 + 2t^2 + 1)/(4ts). Then compute (a-X)w + X(b-h_y) = - (st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2)/(8 t^2 s). So absolute value = (st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2)/(8 t^2 s). And |v| = sqrt(X^2+w^2) = sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/(2t). Thus d = (st-1) sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/(4 t s).

- Compute radius R_c of circle (BEF) = distance from O to B = sqrt(a^2+(b+1)^2). Compute a^2+(b+1)^2 = (st-1)^2/(16 t^2 s^2) (1+s^2)(1+t^2). So R_c = (st-1) sqrt((1+s^2)(1+t^2))/(4 t s) = d.

- Hence L is tangent to circle (BEF). QED.

We'll need to present the derivation of a and b more concretely. We can do:

From |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2:

a^2 + (b+1)^2 = (a - λ_E X)^2 + (b - (1-λ_E w))^2 = a^2 -2a λ_E X + λ_E^2 X^2 + b^2 -2b(1-λ_E w) + (1-λ_E w)^2.

Cancel a^2+b^2:

2b+1 = -2a λ_E X + λ_E^2 X^2 -2b(1-λ_E w) + (1-λ_E w)^2.

Similarly, with μ.

Then subtract the two equations to eliminate a? Actually, we can subtract to get relation between b and λ,μ. That gave 2w(b-1) = - (λ+μ)K + 2aX. But that involves a. Instead, we can solve for a from one equation and then substitute into the other. But we already derived b using subtraction of the two equations after dividing by (λ-μ). That gave b = - (λμK)/4. Let's derive that carefully.

We have two equations:

2b+1 = λ^2K -2aλX -2b(1-λw) + (1-λw)^2? Wait, let's rewrite the condition |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 leads to:

a^2+(b+1)^2 = (a-λX)^2 + (b-(1-λw))^2.

Expand right: a^2 -2aλX + λ^2X^2 + b^2 -2b(1-λw) + (1-λw)^2. Cancel a^2+b^2:

2b+1 = -2aλX + λ^2X^2 -2b(1-λw) + (1-λw)^2.

Now, (1-λw)^2 = 1 -2λw + λ^2w^2. So:

2b+1 = -2aλX + λ^2X^2 -2b +2bλw +1 -2λw + λ^2w^2.

Cancel 1 on both sides:

2b = -2aλX + λ^2X^2 -2b +2bλw -2λw + λ^2w^2.

Bring -2b to left: 2b+2b = 4b = -2aλX + λ^2X^2 +2bλw -2λw + λ^2w^2.

Thus:

4b = -2aλX + λ^2(X^2+w^2) + 2λw(b-1) = -2aλX + λ^2K + 2λw(b-1).   (1)

Similarly, with μ:

4b = -2aμX + μ^2K + 2μw(b-1).   (2)

Subtract (1) from (2):

0 = -2aX(μ-λ) + (μ^2-λ^2)K + 2w(b-1)(μ-λ) = (μ-λ)[ -2aX + (μ+λ)K + 2w(b-1) ].

Since μ≠λ, we get:

-2aX + (μ+λ)K + 2w(b-1) = 0 => 2w(b-1) = 2aX - (μ+λ)K.   (C)

Now, from (1): 4b = -2aλX + λ^2K + 2λw(b-1). Substitute (C) into (1): 4b = -2aλX + λ^2K + 2λw(b-1). But we have 2w(b-1) = 2aX - (μ+λ)K, so 2λw(b-1) = λ(2aX - (μ+λ)K) = 2aλX - λ(μ+λ)K. Then (1) becomes: 4b = -2aλX + λ^2K + 2aλX - λ(μ+λ)K = λ^2K - λμK - λ^2K = -λμK. So 4b = -λμK => b = -λμK/4. Good.

Thus b = -λμK/4.

Now, compute λ, μ, K in terms of s,t. We have:

λ = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2), μ = 2t(t+1/s)/(1+t^2), K = (1+s^2)(1+t^2)/(4t^2).

So λμK = [2(1+st)/(1+s^2)] * [2t(t+1/s)/(1+t^2)] * [(1+s^2)(1+t^2)/(4t^2)] = 4t(1+st)(t+1/s)/(4t^2) = (1+st)(t+1/s)/t. Then b = - (1+st)(t+1/s)/(4t). That's the expression.

Now, we need a. We can use equation (1) or (2). Use (1): 4b = -2aλX + λ^2K + 2λw(b-1). So 2aλX = λ^2K + 2λw(b-1) - 4b. Divide by 2λ: aX = (λK)/2 + w(b-1) - (2b)/λ. Then a = [ (λK)/2 + w(b-1) - (2b)/λ ] / X. Substitute expressions. Alternatively, use the derived relation (C) to express a in terms of b? Actually, from (C): 2w(b-1) = 2aX - (μ+λ)K => aX = w(b-1) + (μ+λ)K/2. So a = [ w(b-1) + (μ+λ)K/2 ] / X. That might be easier.

We already computed a using the bracket method, which gave a = (s+t)(st-1)/(4ts). Let's compute directly using this formula.

We have X = (st-1)/(2t), w = (t+s)/(2t), K = (1+s^2)(1+t^2)/(4t^2). We also have b = - (1+st)(t+1/s)/(4t). Compute b-1 = - (1+st)(t+1/s)/(4t) - 1 = -[(1+st)(t+1/s) + 4t]/(4t). We can simplify later.

So the derivation of a using the bracket method is correct. We can present that derivation concisely by noting that from the expressions we can compute the bracket and simplify using the identities.

Given the solution, we can present the derivation of a as:

Compute λ^2K - 4b - 2λw(1-b). Using λ = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2), K = (1+s^2)(1+t^2)/(4t^2), b = -(1+st)(t+1/s)/(4t), w = (t+s)/(2t). After simplification, we obtain:

λ^2K - 4b - 2λw(1-b) = (s+t)(1-st)^2/(2t^2s). Then aX = (λ^2K - 4b - 2λw(1-b))/(2λ) = (s+t)(1-st)^2/(4t^2s(1+st))? Wait, we need to be careful.

We have from (1): 4b = -2aλX + λ^2K + 2λw(b-1). So 2aλX = λ^2K + 2λw(b-1) - 4b. Thus aX = (λ^2K + 2λw(b-1) - 4b)/(2λ). Then a = [ (λ^2K + 2λw(b-1) - 4b)/(2λ) ] / X. Since X = (st-1)/(2t), we have a = [ (λ^2K + 2λw(b-1) - 4b)/(2λ) ] * (2t/(st-1)) = t (λ^2K + 2λw(b-1) - 4b)/(λ(st-1)). Now, we compute the numerator N1 = λ^2K + 2λw(b-1) - 4b. Using the expressions, we can simplify N1. Let's do that:

λ^2K = [4(1+st)^2/(1+s^2)^2] * [(1+s^2)(1+t^2)/(4t^2)] = (1+st)^2 (1+t^2)/(t^2 (1+s^2)).
2λw(b-1) = 2 * [2(1+st)/(1+s^2)] * [(t+s)/(2t)] * (b-1) = [4(1+st)(t+s)/(2t(1+s^2))] * (b-1)? Actually, 2λw = 2 * 2(1+st)/(1+s^2) * (t+s)/(2t) = 2(1+st)(t+s)/(t(1+s^2)). So 2λw(b-1) = 2(1+st)(t+s)/(t(1+s^2)) * (b-1).
4b = 4 * [ -(1+st)(t+1/s)/(4t) ] = -(1+st)(t+1/s)/t.
Then N1 = λ^2K + 2λw(b-1) - 4b = (1+st)^2 (1+t^2)/(t^2 (1+s^2)) + 2(1+st)(t+s)/(t(1+s^2)) (b-1) + (1+st)(t+1/s)/t.

Now, we need to express (b-1) in terms of s,t. b = -(1+st)(t+1/s)/(4t). So b-1 = - (1+st)(t+1/s)/(4t) - 1 = -[(1+st)(t+1/s) + 4t]/(4t). Compute (1+st)(t+1/s) = t + 1/s + s t^2 + 1. So plus 4t gives 5t + 1/s + s t^2 + 1. So b-1 = -(5t + 1/s + s t^2 + 1)/(4t). Then 2(1+st)(t+s)/(t(1+s^2)) (b-1) = 2(1+st)(t+s)/(t(1+s^2)) * [ -(5t + 1/s + s t^2 + 1)/(4t) ] = - (1+st)(t+s)(5t + 1/s + s t^2 + 1) / (2 t^2 (1+s^2)).

Thus N1 = (1+st)^2 (1+t^2)/(t^2 (1+s^2)) - (1+st)(t+s)(5t + 1/s + s t^2 + 1)/(2 t^2 (1+s^2)) + (1+st)(t+1/s)/t.

Put everything over common denominator 2 t^2 (1+s^2). Write:

Second term: - (1+st)(t+s)(5t + 1/s + s t^2 + 1) / (2 t^2 (1+s^2)).
Third term: (1+st)(t+1/s)/t = (1+st)(t+1/s) * 2 t (1+s^2) / (2 t^2 (1+s^2))? Actually, to combine, write third term as something over 2 t^2 (1+s^2). Multiply numerator and denominator by 2 t (1+s^2): (1+st)(t+1/s)/t = (1+st)(t+1/s) * 2 t (1+s^2) / (2 t^2 (1+s^2))? That would be (1+st)(t+1/s) * 2 t (1+s^2) / (2 t^2 (1+s^2)) = (1+st)(t+1/s) * 2 (1+s^2) / (2 t (1+s^2)? No, we need denominator 2 t^2 (1+s^2). So multiply numerator and denominator by 2 t (1+s^2): (1+st)(t+1/s)/t = (1+st)(t+1/s) * 2 t (1+s^2) / (2 t^2 (1+s^2))? Actually, (1+st)(t+1/s)/t = (1+st)(t+1/s) * (2 t (1+s^2)) / (2 t^2 (1+s^2))? That would be (1+st)(t+1/s) * 2 t (1+s^2) / (2 t^2 (1+s^2)) = (1+st)(t+1/s) * 2 (1+s^2) / (2 t (1+s^2))? This is messy. Instead, note that the third term is simpler: (1+st)(t+1/s)/t = (1+st)(t+1/s)/t. To combine with denominator 2 t^2 (1+s^2), we write:

(1+st)(t+1/s)/t = [ (1+st)(t+1/s) * 2 t (1+s^2) ] / [ 2 t^2 (1+s^2) ]? Actually, that would give numerator (1+st)(t+1/s) * 2 t (1+s^2). But we need numerator to be something like? Then the third term becomes [2 t (1+s^2) (1+st)(t+1/s)] / [2 t^2 (1+s^2)] = [2 (1+s^2) (1+st)(t+1/s)] / [2 t (1+s^2)]? That's not correct because denominator is 2 t^2 (1+s^2), so to get that denominator, we multiply numerator and denominator by 2 t (1+s^2): (1+st)(t+1/s)/t = [ (1+st)(t+1/s) * 2 t (1+s^2) ] / [ 2 t^2 (1+s^2) ]. So indeed, the third term can be written as [2 t (1+s^2) (1+st)(t+1/s)] / [2 t^2 (1+s^2)] = [2 (1+s^2) (1+st)(t+1/s)] / [2 t (1+s^2)]? Wait, the denominator is 2 t^2 (1+s^2), so after multiplication, we have numerator 2 t (1+s^2) (1+st)(t+1/s), denominator 2 t^2 (1+s^2). So the fraction simplifies to [2 (1+st)(t+1/s)] / [2 t]? No, because (1+s^2) cancels? Actually, we have numerator: 2 t (1+s^2) (1+st)(t+1/s); denominator: 2 t^2 (1+s^2). Cancel (1+s^2) and one t: gives 2 (1+st)(t+1/s) / (2 t) = (1+st)(t+1/s)/t. So it's the same. So this is not helpful. We need to express the third term with denominator 2 t^2 (1+s^2) but then numerator becomes 2 t (1+s^2) (1+st)(t+1/s). That is fine. So N1 = [2 (1+st)^2 (1+t^2) - (1+st)(t+s)(5t + 1/s + s t^2 + 1) + 2 t (1+s^2) (1+st)(t+1/s)] / [2 t^2 (1+s^2)]. Now, we can factor (1+st). Then N1 = (1+st) [ 2 (1+st)(1+t^2) - (t+s)(5t + 1/s + s t^2 + 1) + 2 t (1+s^2)(t+1/s) ] / [2 t^2 (1+s^2)]. Now, simplify the bracket B = 2 (1+st)(1+t^2) - (t+s)(5t + 1/s + s t^2 + 1) + 2 t (1+s^2)(t+1/s). Compute each term:

First term: 2 (1+st)(1+t^2) = 2(1+st)(1+t^2) = 2(1+t^2) + 2st(1+t^2) = 2+2t^2+2st+2s t^3.

Second term: (t+s)(5t + 1/s + s t^2 + 1) = we already expanded: = 5t^2+5st+t/s+1+s t^3+s^2 t^2+t+s. So with minus sign: - (t+s)(...) = -5t^2 -5st - t/s -1 - s t^3 - s^2 t^2 - t - s.

Third term: 2 t (1+s^2)(t+1/s) = 2 t (1+s^2)(t+1/s) = 2 t [(1+s^2)t + (1+s^2)/s] = 2 t [t + s^2 t + 1/s + s] = 2 t [t + s^2 t + 1/s + s] = 2t^2 + 2 t s^2 t? Actually, compute: 2 t * (t) = 2t^2; 2 t * (s^2 t) = 2 s^2 t^2; 2 t * (1/s) = 2t/s; 2 t * (s) = 2 t s. So third term = 2t^2 + 2 s^2 t^2 + 2t/s + 2 t s.

Now, sum B = first + second + third = (2+2t^2+2st+2s t^3) + (-5t^2 -5st - t/s -1 - s t^3 - s^2 t^2 - t - s) + (2t^2 + 2 s^2 t^2 + 2t/s + 2 t s). Combine:

Constants: 2 -1 = 1.
t^2: 2t^2 -5t^2 +2t^2 = -t^2.
st: 2st -5st +2ts = -st? Actually 2st -5st +2st = -st. Yes.
s t^3: 2s t^3 - s t^3 = s t^3.
s^2 t^2: - s^2 t^2 + 2 s^2 t^2 = s^2 t^2.
t/s: -t/s + 2t/s = t/s.
t: -t (from second) +? first has no t, third has no t, so -t.
s: -s (from second) +? first has no s, third has no s, so -s.

Thus B = 1 - t^2 - st + t/s + s t^3 + s^2 t^2 - t - s. That is exactly the same as N we had earlier. So B = N. Then N1 = (1+st) * B / [2 t^2 (1+s^2)] = (1+st) * (s+t)(1-st)^2/(2t^2 s) * 1/(1+s^2)? Wait, we have B = (s+t)(1-st)^2/s? Actually, we previously found that N = (s+t)(1-st)^2/s. So B = (s+t)(1-st)^2/s. Then N1 = (1+st) * (s+t)(1-st)^2/(s) / [2 t^2 (1+s^2)]. So N1 = (s+t)(1-st)^2 (1+st) / [2 t^2 s (1+s^2)]. Now, we want aX = N1/(2λ). λ = 2(1+st)/(1+s^2). So 2λ = 4(1+st)/(1+s^2). Then aX = N1 / (2λ) = [ (s+t)(1-st)^2 (1+st) / (2 t^2 s (1+s^2)) ] * [ (1+s^2)/(4(1+st)) ] = (s+t)(1-st)^2 / (8 t^2 s). So aX = (s+t)(st-1)^2/(8 t^2 s). Since (1-st)^2 = (st-1)^2.

Now, X = (st-1)/(2t). So a = (aX)/X = [ (s+t)(st-1)^2/(8 t^2 s) ] / [ (st-1)/(2t) ] = (s+t)(st-1)^2/(8 t^2 s) * (2t)/(st-1) = (s+t)(st-1)/(4 t s). So a = (s+t)(st-1)/(4 t s). That's the expression. Good.

So we can present this derivation concisely.

Now, the proof is complete.

Now, we need to write the solution in a clear, organized way. Since the problem is about geometry, we should use geometric language and then coordinates. We'll start with coordinate setup.

I'll write the solution step by step.

We also need to note that we assume the configuration is such that AP is not the common chord, so that E and F are distinct from B, and the circumcircle of BEF is defined. The problem statement likely implies that E and F are distinct from A and B, so we can safely assume that.

Now, produce the final XML.

We'll include the solution inside <solution> tags.

We'll also include a brief self-evaluation.

I'll write the solution in a rigorous manner.

Let's start:

<solution>
We set up a coordinate system. Let the common chord $AB$ be the $y$-axis, and the line of centers $MN$ be the $x$-axis. Since the configuration is scale-invariant, we may scale so that $A=(0,1)$ and $B=(0,-1)$. Let $M=(m,0)$ and $N=(n,0)$ with $m<n$ (the order $C,M,N,D$ on $MN$ forces $m<n$). The circles are then
\[
\Omega:\;(x-m)^2+y^2=m^2+1,\qquad \Gamma:\;(x-n)^2+y^2=n^2+1.
\]

We introduce hyperbolic parameters: let $m=\sinh u$, $n=\sinh v$. Because $\sinh$ is increasing, $u<v$. Set $t=e^u>0$ and $s=e^v>0$, so $t<s$. Then
\[
r=\cosh u=\frac{t+1/t}{2},\qquad R=\cosh v=\frac{s+1/s}{2},
\]
and the points $C$ and $D$ are the second intersections of $MN$ with the circles:
\[
C=(m-r,0)=(-e^{-u},0)=\left(-\frac1t,0\right),\qquad D=(n+R,0)=(e^v,0)=(s,0).
\]

The circumcenter $P$ of $\triangle ACD$ lies on the perpendicular bisector of $CD$, which is the vertical line $x=\frac{s-1/t}{2}$. Write $X=\frac{s-1/t}{2}$. Let $P=(X,Y)$. From $PA=PC$ we obtain
\[
X^2+(Y-1)^2 = (X+1/t)^2+Y^2.
\]
Simplifying gives $Y=\frac{1-s/t}{2}$. Hence
\[
P=\left(\frac{s-1/t}{2},\,\frac{1-s/t}{2}\right).
\]
Notice that $Y-1=-\frac{1+s/t}{2}$.

The line $AP$ has direction vector $\mathbf{v}=(X,\;Y-1)=\left(\frac{s-1/t}{2},\;-\frac{1+s/t}{2}\right)$. Write $w=\frac{1+s/t}{2}$, so $\mathbf{v}=(X,-w)$. Also $X=\frac{st-1}{2t}$ and $w=\frac{t+s}{2t}$.

Now find the second intersections $E$ and $F$ of $AP$ with $\Omega$ and $\Gamma$. Parameterize $AP$ as $(0,1)+\lambda\mathbf{v}$. Substituting into $\Omega$:
\[
\left(\lambda X-m\right)^2+(\lambda(-w)+1)^2 = m^2+1.
\]
Using $m=\frac{t-1/t}{2}$ and simplifying yields
\[
\lambda=0\quad\text{or}\quad \lambda=\frac{2(1+st)}{1+s^2}.
\]
Thus $E$ corresponds to $\lambda_E=\frac{2(1+st)}{1+s^2}$. Similarly, substituting into $\Gamma$ gives
\[
\lambda_F=\frac{2t(t+1/s)}{1+t^2}.
\]
Hence
\[
E=\left(\lambda_E X,\;1-\lambda_E w\right),\qquad F=\left(\lambda_F X,\;1-\lambda_F w\right).
\]

Let $O=(a,b)$ be the circumcenter of $\triangle BEF$ with $B=(0,-1)$. From $|OB|^2=|OE|^2$ and $|OB|^2=|OF|^2$ we obtain
\[
a^2+(b+1)^2=(a-\lambda_E X)^2+(b-1+\lambda_E w)^2,\qquad
a^2+(b+1)^2=(a-\lambda_F X)^2+(b-1+\lambda_F w)^2.
\]
Expanding and simplifying, the two equations become
\[
4b = -2a\lambda_E X+\lambda_E^2K+2\lambda_E w(b-1),\qquad
4b = -2a\lambda_F X+\lambda_F^2K+2\lambda_F w(b-1),
\]
where $K=X^2+w^2$. Subtracting the two equations and using $\lambda_E\neq\lambda_F$ leads to
\[
-2aX+(\lambda_E+\lambda_F)K+2w(b-1)=0.\tag{1}
\]
Solving (1) together with the first equation for $b$ gives
\[
4b = -\lambda_E\lambda_F K\quad\Longrightarrow\quad b = -\frac{\lambda_E\lambda_F K}{4}.
\]
Inserting the expressions $\lambda_E=\frac{2(1+st)}{1+s^2}$, $\lambda_F=\frac{2t(t+1/s)}{1+t^2}$, $K=\frac{(1+s^2)(1+t^2)}{4t^2}$ yields
\[
\lambda_E\lambda_F K = \frac{(1+st)(t+1/s)}{t},\qquad
b = -\frac{(1+st)(t+1/s)}{4t}.
\]

Now from (1) we have $aX = w(b-1)+\frac{(\lambda_E+\lambda_F)K}{2}$. Using the known values,
\[
w = \frac{t+s}{2t},\quad b-1 = -\frac{(1+st)(t+1/s)+4t}{4t} = -\frac{5t+1/s+st^2+1}{4t},
\]
and after algebraic simplification (or directly by the earlier elimination) we obtain
\[
a = \frac{(s+t)(st-1)}{4ts}.
\]

Thus the circumcircle of $\triangle BEF$ has center $O=(a,b)$ and radius $R_c=\sqrt{a^2+(b+1)^2}$.

Now compute the orthocenter $H$ of $\triangle PMN$. Points $P=(X,Y)$, $M=(\frac{t-1/t}{2},0)$, $N=(\frac{s-1/s}{2},0)$. Because $M$ and $N$ lie on the $x$-axis, the altitude from $P$ to $MN$ is the vertical line $x=X$. The altitude from $M$ to $PN$ is the line through $M$ perpendicular to $PN$. The vector $PN$ is $(n-X,\,-Y)$. A direct computation gives the intersection of this altitude with $x=X$ as
\[
H=\left(X,\;\frac{(X-m)(n-X)}{Y}\right).
\]
Substituting $X=\frac{st-1}{2t}$, $m=\frac{t-1/t}{2}$, $n=\frac{s-1/s}{2}$, $Y=\frac{1-st}{2t}$ simplifies $H$ to
\[
H=\left(\frac{st-1}{2t},\;\frac{t-s}{2s}\right).
\]

The line through $H$ parallel to $AP$ has direction $\mathbf{v}=(X,-w)$. Its equation is
\[
(x,y) = \left(X,\;\frac{t-s}{2s}\right) + u\,(X,-w).
\]

We need to show that this line is tangent to the circumcircle of $\triangle BEF$. The distance from $O$ to this line is
\[
d = \frac{|\det(O-H,\mathbf{v})|}{|\mathbf{v}|} = \frac{|(a-X)(-w)-(b-\frac{t-s}{2s})X|}{|\mathbf{v}|}
= \frac{|(a-X)w + X(b-\frac{t-s}{2s})|}{|\mathbf{v}|}.
\]
Compute $a-X$ and $b-\frac{t-s}{2s}$ using the expressions found:
\[
a-X = \frac{(s+t)(st-1)}{4ts} - \frac{st-1}{2t} = \frac{(st-1)(t-s)}{4ts},
\]
\[
b-\frac{t-s}{2s} = -\frac{(1+st)(t+1/s)}{4t} - \frac{t-s}{2s} = -\frac{(st-1)^2}{4ts} - \frac{t-s}{2s} = -\frac{(s^2 t^2+2t^2+1)}{4ts}.
\]
Then
\[
(a-X)w + X\left(b-\frac{t-s}{2s}\right) = \frac{(st-1)(t-s)}{4ts}\cdot\frac{t+s}{2t} + \frac{st-1}{2t}\cdot\left(-\frac{s^2 t^2+2t^2+1}{4ts}\right)
= \frac{(st-1)(t-s)(t+s)}{8t^2 s} - \frac{(st-1)(s^2 t^2+2t^2+1)}{8t^2 s}.
\]
Since $(t-s)(t+s)=t^2-s^2$, the numerator simplifies to
\[
(st-1)\bigl(t^2-s^2 - s^2 t^2 - 2t^2 -1\bigr) = -(st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2).
\]
Hence
\[
(a-X)w + X\left(b-\frac{t-s}{2s}\right) = -\frac{(st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2)}{8t^2 s}.
\]
Taking absolute value,
\[
|(a-X)w + X(b-\frac{t-s}{2s})| = \frac{(st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2)}{8t^2 s}.
\]

Now $|\mathbf{v}| = \sqrt{X^2+w^2} = \sqrt{\frac{(1+s^2)(1+t^2)}{4t^2}} = \frac{\sqrt{(1+s^2)(1+t^2)}}{2t}$. Therefore
\[
d = \frac{(st-1)(1+t^2)(1+s^2)}{8t^2 s} \cdot \frac{2t}{\sqrt{(1+s^2)(1+t^2)}} = \frac{(st-1)\sqrt{(1+s^2)(1+t^2)}}{4ts}.
\]

On the other hand,
\[
R_c^2 = a^2+(b+1)^2 = \left(\frac{(s+t)(st-1)}{4ts}\right)^2 + \left(-\frac{(st-1)^2}{4ts}\right)^2 = \frac{(st-1)^2}{16t^2 s^2}\bigl((s+t)^2+(st-1)^2\bigr).
\]
But $(s+t)^2+(st-1)^2 = (1+s^2)(1+t^2)$, so
\[
R_c = \frac{(st-1)\sqrt{(1+s^2)(1+t^2)}}{4ts} = d.
\]

Thus the distance from $O$ to the line through $H$ parallel to $AP$ equals the radius of the circumcircle of $\triangle BEF$, proving that the line is tangent to that circle. This completes the proof.
</solution>
